==PAGE:6==
ABSTRACT
A detailed investigation of to what degree optical ﬁbres can be used in connection with
fault detection in submarine power cables is presented. The submarine power cable used
as the basis for the investigation is a 630 km subsea power cable between Great Britain
and Denmark, named VikingLink.
This investigation was performed by the use of literature studies and analytical approaches
to promising solutions. It was found that optical ﬁbres can be used in connection with
fault detection in submarine power cables.
The most promising fault detection method for VikingLink, under the assumption that if
the power cable breaks, so does the optical ﬁbre, is the EXFO FTB-7600-E UHD OTDR.
None of the temperature sensing methods investigated have showed the ability to reach
the length of the VikingLink, nor has any of the strain sensor methods investigated.
The vibration sensor method investigated cannot cover the reach of the VikingLink, but
indications of interesting developments are expected in the future. The optical ﬁbre as a
magnetic ﬁeld sensor is, with the technology today, not recommended for subsea power
cables.
Additionally proof of concept experiments are performed to investigate an OTDR and
temperature sensing by the use of Brillouin scattering. The OTDR showed expected
result for measuring the length, ﬁbre type and position of bend of the optical ﬁbre. The
temperature sensing method by the use of Brillouin scattering showed a linear relationship
between the Brillouin shift in MHz and the change in optical ﬁbre temperature.

1
INTRODUCTION
Energinet plans a 1400 MW, 525 kV, high voltage direct current interconnection between
Great Britain and Denmark, named VikingLink. The project is being jointly developed and
funded by National Grid and Energinet. The interconnection will consist of two submarine
and underground cables and two converter stations. The length of the connection is 760 km
(630 km subsea) between Great Britain and Denmark, [1].The interconnection is named
VikingLink and it is on the list of important infrastructure projects that contribute to the
interconnection of the European power grid, the Projects of Common Interest (PCI) list, [2].
Submarine cables of this length can be expected to experience several faults through their
lifetime and therefore monitoring and fault detection of such cable systems are of great
importance.
In this report the question of whether optical ﬁbres can be used in connection with fault
detection in submarine power cables is investigated.
This is done by the use of literature reviews and calculations. Some of the ﬁnding are
further investigated experimentally under laboratory conditions.
As a result, it is found that optical ﬁbres can be used for fault detection and
recommendations for fault detection and monitoring of the VikingLink are presented.
1
==PAGE:12==
2
BACKGROUND INFO REGARDING OPTICAL
FIBRES
In this chapter a brief description of the physical concepts, as well as the optical components
needed for monitoring and fault detection of submarine cables with optical ﬁbres, is
presented.
2.1
Geometrical Description of Optical Fibres
In this section a geometrical description of the step-index ﬁbre is presented. A geometrical
description of the graded-index ﬁbre is presented in appendix A subsection A.1.2. The
geometrical-optics description is approximative, but valid, when the core radius a is much
larger than the light wavelength λ.
2.1.1
Step-Index Fibre
The simplest form of an optical ﬁbre is a cylindrical silica glass core surrounded by a
cladding with a lower refractive index than the core. A step-index ﬁbre is such a ﬁbre with
an abrupt index change in the core-cladding interference, as seen in ﬁgure 2.1.
Figure 2.1: Cross section and refractive-index proﬁle for a step-index ﬁbre.
Figure from [3].
The refractive index for the core is higher than the refractive index for the cladding, n1 > n2.
2
==PAGE:13==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
When considering a ray incident on the core center, as seen in ﬁgure 2.2, making an
angle θi with the ﬁbre end, the ray will bend towards the normal, due to refraction at
the ﬁbre-air interference, forming the refracted angle, θR of the refracted ray, given by
n0sinθi = n1sinθR, where n0 is the refractive index of the air. The refracted ray will hit the
core-cladding interference and then be refracted again. But the refraction is only possible
for an angle of incidence φ, so that φ < n2/n1. If the angle is larger than the critical
angle, φc, deﬁned as sinφc = n2/n1, the ray will experience total internal reﬂection at the
core-cladding interference. All rays with φ > φc will remain conﬁned in the ﬁbre core.
Figure 2.2: Light conﬁnement through total internal reﬂection of a guided ray
in a step-index ﬁbre, ﬁgure from [4].
2.2
Wave Propagation
In this section the propagation of light in a step-index ﬁbre is described using Maxwell’s
equations for electromagnetic waves. The equations are solved in order to obtain the
optical modes that are guided inside the ﬁbre.
2.2.1
Maxwell’s Equations
The propagation of optical ﬁelds in step-index ﬁbres are governed by Maxwell’s equations
and they are presented in appendix A subsection A.2.1.
From Maxwell’s equations the wave equation can be deduced. The wave equation for the
electric ﬁeld will take the form of
∇2E = 1
c2
∂2E
∂t2
(2.1)
2.2.2
Fibre Modes
An optical mode is a solution to the wave equation, equation 2.1, that satisﬁes the boundary
conditions and does not change spatial distribution upon propagation, [3]. In appendix
A subsection A.2.2, the calculations of the optical ﬁbre modes in step-index ﬁbres are
presented. From appendix A subsection A.2.2 the normalized frequency is given by
V = 2πa
λ
q
n2
1 −n2
2
(2.2)
3
==PAGE:14==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
A ﬁbre with a large value of V supports many modes. For the value of V = 2.405 only
the fundamental mode is supported in the ﬁbre, and ﬁbres only supporting this mode are
know as singlemode ﬁbre (SMF).
An elaboration on the singlemode cutoﬀvalue is presented in appendix A subsection A.2.2.
2.2.3
Fibre Birefringence
An ideal singlemode ﬁbre has a perfectly cylindrical core with a uniform diameter. Real
ﬁbres do exhibit variations in the shape of the core along the ﬁbre length. They also
experience nonuniform stress that can break the cylindrical symmetry of the ﬁbre. This
will cause the modal index in the x- and y-direction, in Cartesian coordinates, of the
ﬁbre to be slightly diﬀerent from one another, as neffx and neffy. Where neffx and neffy
are the mode indices for the orthogonally polarized ﬁbre modes. The degree of model
birefringence is deﬁned as, [3]
Bm = |neffx −neffy|
(2.3)
Birefringence will lead to a periodic exchange of power between the two polarized
components. The period is called the beat length and is given by LB = λ/Bm.
Linearly polarized light remains linearly polarized when it is polarized along one of the
principal axes. If not, its state of polarization will change along the length of the ﬁbre,
from linearly polarized to elliptically polarized and back to linearly polarized, in a periodic
manner over the length LB.
In ﬁgure 2.3 such a periodical change in the state of polarization for a ﬁbre of constant
birefringence B is schematically shown. In the ﬁgure the fast axis is the axis along which
the mode index is smaller. The other axis is called the slow axis.
Figure 2.3: State of polarization in a birefringent ﬁbre over one beat length.
The input beam is linearly polarized at 45 o with respect to the slow and fast axes.
Figure from [3].
In singlemode ﬁbres the birefringence is not constant along the ﬁbre, but it changes
randomly, both in magnitude and in direction, due to the variations in the core shape and
the anisotropic stress acting on the core. As a result, linearly polarized light, launched into
the ﬁbre, will reach a state of arbitrary polarization after a short transmission. Furthermore,
diﬀerent frequency components of a pulse acquire diﬀerent polarization states, thus resulting
in broadening of the pulse, known as polarisationmode dispersion (PMD), as presented in
4
==PAGE:15==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
A.3.5 . This can be overcome by polarization-maintaining ﬁbres (PM), where the random
ﬂuctuations in the core shape and size are not the governing factors in determining the state
of polarization. In these ﬁbres a large amount of birefringence is introduced intentionally
in the design of the ﬁbre, so that small random birefringence ﬂuctuations do not aﬀect the
polarization of the light signiﬁcantly.
2.2.4
Basic Propagation Equation
The basic propagation equation that governs a pulse evolution inside a SMF will be
presented.
The wave equation from equation 2.1 can be presented in terms of frequency, [4] as
∇2 ˜E + n2(ω)k2
0 ˜E = 0
(2.4)
and is used to determine the ﬁbre modes.
The optical ﬁeld in frequency, that propagates in a singlemode ﬁbre can be described as,
˜E(r, ω) = ˆxF(x, y) ˜B(0, ω)exp(iβz)
(2.5)
with ˆx as the polarization unit vector and ˜B(0, ω) as the initial amplitude. The ﬁeld
distribution F(x, y) of the fundamental ﬁbre mode can be assumed Gaussian distributed
[3]. Diﬀerent spectral components of the optical signal will propagate, inside the ﬁbre, as
˜B(z, ω) = ˜B(0, ω)exp(iβz)
(2.6)
The temporal propagation through the ﬁbre will be investigated.

domain. The initial spectral amplitude ˜B(0, ω) is the Fourier transform of the input
amplitude B(0, t) as presented in equation A.17 in appendix A subsection A.2.3.
The diﬀerent spectral components of the optical signal will propagate through the ﬁbre as
given in equation 2.6. An inverse Fourier transformation is need to obtain the amplitude
back in time domain

2.3
Dispersion
Dispersion results in pulse broadening. Diﬀerent spectral components of a pulse travelling
through an optical ﬁbre, will travel at slightly diﬀerent group velocities, this is known
as group-velocity dispersion (GVD), intramodal dispersion or simply ﬁbre dispersion, [3].
Intramodal dispersion is made up oﬀtwo contributions; material dispersion and waveguide
dispersion.
The fault detection methods utilizing scattering, as presented in section 2.10 through 2.13
all transmits a single pulse at a time and therefore dispersion of a possible pulse-train
will not be an issue. Dispersion of the pulse width could inﬂuence the dead zone of the
detection, but this is assumed calibrated in the fault detection products on the market.
The fault detection method utilizing magnetic ﬁled sensing, as presented in subsection 2.14
uses linear polarized continuous laser light where the position of the laser wavelength is
not of interest.
In appendix A subsection A.3 dispersion is presented in detail.
6
==PAGE:17==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
2.4
Losses
Fibre losses reduce the power of the signal transmitted.
In this section various loss
mechanisms in optical ﬁbres are described.
2.4.1
Attenuation Coeﬃcient
Usually the average optical power P of a beam propagating in an optical ﬁbre is governed
by Beer’s law, [3], which is dP/dz = −αP, where α is the attenuation coeﬃcient. Letting
Pin be the launched power into a ﬁbre of length L, the output power, Pout, can be expressed
as
Pout = Pinexp(−αL)
(2.13)
It is custom to express the attenuation coeﬃcient in dB/km, by using the relation presented
in equation A.56.
The ﬁbre loss depends on the transmitted wavelength. The loss spectrum α(λ) for a
singlemode ﬁbre is presented in appendix A subsection A.4.1. The lowest region of loss is
at the wavelength region near 1550 nm of only 0.2 dB/km.
The loss mechanism of material absorption can be divided into two categories, intrinsic
absorption and extrinsic absorption, both presented in appendix A subsection A.4.2.
2.4.2
Macro-Bending
When an optical ﬁbre is bend, the radius may restrain the light ray from hitting the
core-cladding interface at an angle greater than the critical angle. The light ray will
therefore not experience total internal reﬂection. Some of the light will refract into the
cladding. An illustration of macro-bending of the optical ﬁbre is seen in ﬁgure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Illustration of bending radius and loss in macro-bending. Figure
from [5].
The bending loss is proportional to exp(−R/Rc), with R as the radius of the macro bend
and Rc typically takes values between 0.2 µm and 0.4 µm for singlemode ﬁbres and is
deﬁned as
Rc =
a
n2
1 −n2
2
(2.14)
The bending loss is negligible (<0.1 dB/km) for bend radius R > 5 mm.
7
==PAGE:18==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
2.5
Scattering
In general three diﬀerent scattering processes can be observed when injecting an optical
signal into an optical ﬁbre, [5], as seen in ﬁgure 2.5.
Figure 2.5: Illustration of scattering through an optical ﬁbre. Figure from [5].
Rayleigh scattering is backwards scattering at the same wavelength as the signal. Both
Raman and Brillouin scattering create Stokes and Anit-Stokes components. Brillouin only
backscatter where Raman scatter both in forward and backward direction.
2.5.1
Rayleigh Scattering
Rayleigh scattering is caused by small variations in the density of the glass as a result of
the production of the optical ﬁbre. The size of these variations are <λ and scatter the
light in all directions, as seen in ﬁgure 2.6.
Figure 2.6: Rayleigh scattering due to density variation in the glass material.
Figure from [5].

2.5.2
Brillouin Scattering
Brillouin scattering is due to electrostriction, meaning the tendency of a material to become
compressed in the presence of an electric ﬁeld. The light travelling inside an optical ﬁbre
is an oscillating ﬁeld which will induce a moving refractive index grating, that in return
generates new frequencies. This is shown schematically in ﬁgure 2.9.
Figure 2.9: Schematic illustration of electrostriction and how the oscillating
ﬁeld will induce a moving refractive index grating, that in return will generate
new frequencies. Figure from [4].
10
==PAGE:21==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
The level of Brillouin scattering depends on the power of the incoming oscillating ﬁeld.
The scattering generates a frequency down-shifted component (Stokes) and a frequency
up-shifted component (Anti-Stokes) as seen in ﬁgure 2.5.
The threshold power level for stimulated Brillouin scattering can be described as the
input power, Pth = Pp(0), at which the transmitted and reﬂected power become equal,
PSBS = Pp(L). A sketch of the powers included in determining the threshold power level
for stimulated Brillouin scattering for an input pump power Pp(0) over a ﬁbre of length L
with an output power of Pp(L) and a stimulated Brillouin scattering power of PSBS, can
be seen in ﬁgure 2.10.
Figure 2.10: Illustration of the powers included in determining the threshold
power level for stimulated Brillouin scattering for an input pump power Pp(0)
over a ﬁbre of length L with an output power of Pp(L) and a stimulated Brillouin
scattering power of PSBS. Figure from [4].
The threshold power satisﬁes the condition

optical ﬁbre exceeds the threshold power level, most of the light is reﬂected backward
through stimulated Brillouin scattering (SBS). Therefore the SBS limits the launched
power to a few milliwatts because of its low threshold level.
2.5.3
Raman Scattering
Raman scattering occurs due to the interaction of an incoming electrical ﬁeld with the
atoms or molecules in a material. Here the material is the silica glass of the optical ﬁbre and
the electric ﬁeld stems from the incoming photons from the light ray. It can be understood
11
==PAGE:22==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
as the excitation of the electrons in the material up to a virtual energy-level, as seen in
ﬁgure 2.11. When the energy is released, it has another frequency as compared to the
incoming ﬁeld. The process generates both a frequency-down shifted (Stokes) component
and a frequency up-shifted (Anti-Stokes) component, just as for Brillouin scattering, as
seen in ﬁgure 2.5. As opposed to Brillouin scattering, which only scattered in the backward
direction, Raman scattering scatters in both the forward and the backward direction.
Figure 2.11: Raman scattering understood as excitation of the electron in the
material up to a virtual energy-level. When the energy is released it produces
Stokes and Anti-Stokes components. Figure from [4].
Figure 2.11 is simpliﬁed, keeping in mind that the scattering also includes Rayleigh, and
Brillouin scattering also occurs, as seen in ﬁgure 2.5.

2.6
The Optical Fibre in the Presence of a Magnetic Field
In this section the phenomenons, describing the eﬀects on the optical ﬁbre in the presence
of a magnetic ﬁeld, are presented. The optical ﬁbre used for fault detection in submarine
power cables will be in the presence of a magnetic ﬁeld, as the DC power cable will generate
such a ﬁeld.
2.6.1
Magneto-Optics: The Faraday Eﬀect
The Faraday eﬀect is a magneto-optics eﬀect, [6]. Meaning that it is an eﬀect which
happens when an electromagnetic wave, that being the laser light, propagates though a
medium that has been altered due to the presence of a quasi static magnetic ﬁeld. Some
12
==PAGE:23==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
materials can act as natural polarization rotators and their normal modes are waves that
are circularly, rather then linearly, polarized. The angle of rotation is proportional to
the thickness of the material and the strength of magnetic ﬁeld, [7]. The rotation can be
expressed as
θf =
Z
L
BBdl
(2.21)
where B is the Verdet constant, which describes the strength of the Faraday eﬀect for
a particular material and it is both dispersive and temperature-dependent. B is the
component of the magnetic ﬂux density and dl is the diﬀerential vector along the direction
of propagation. The polarization rotation due to a parallel external magnetic ﬁeld on a
magneto-optical material, such as a glass ﬁbre, is seen in ﬁgure 2.12.
Figure 2.12: The Faraday eﬀect in linearly polarized light. Figure from [7].
The rotatory power ρ, which is the rotation angle per unit length, is proportional to the
component of the magnetic ﬂux density B in the direction of the wave propagation as
ρ = BB.
The Faraday eﬀect is diﬀerent from the inherent circular birefringence, as described in
subsection 2.2.3, in the way, that the sign of the Faraday eﬀect depends on the direction of
the magnetic ﬁeld with respect to the direction of propagation of the light. Thus, when a
wave travels through a medium experiencing the Faraday eﬀect, and then reﬂects back
onto itself, travelling once more through the medium in the opposite direction, it undergoes
twice the rotation.
2.7
Fibre Types
The section presents various ﬁbre types currently used for metro, areal, submarine and long-
haul applications. Standardization of the various ﬁbre types is made by the International
Telecommunication Union (ITU), [8].
2.7.1
Non-dispersion Shifted Fibre (ITU-T G.652)
The ITU-T G.652 ﬁbre is also known as the standard SMF and it is the most commonly
deployed ﬁbre. The ﬁbre has a step-index proﬁle and is optimized to operate in the 1310
13
==PAGE:24==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
nm band, but it can also operate in the 1550 nm band. It has a zero-dispersion wavelength
at 1310 nm. The attenuation parameter for G.652 ﬁbre is typically 0.2 dB/km at 1550 nm,
as discussed in subsection 2.4.1, and the polarization mode dispersion (PMD) parameter is
less than 0.1 ps/ km. The PMD is presented in appendix A subsection A.3.5. An example
of this type of ﬁbre is the Corning SMF-28, [9].
Four diﬀerent categories (A, B, C, D) of the G.652 ﬁbre exist. They diﬀer in PMDQ
link design value speciﬁcation, which is a statistical characterization of link PMD, [10]
presented in subsection A.3.5. They also diﬀer in whether the ﬁbres are low water peak
ﬁbres (LWP) with negligible sensitivity to hydrogen exposure or not, [8]. This means that
the water peak is speciﬁed (LWP) or not speciﬁed, which is presented as water peak not
speciﬁed (WPNS), as seen in table 2.1.
Table 2.1: ITU-T G.652 ﬁbre categories.
Category
Maximum PMDQ (ps/km1/2)

2.7.2
Zero Dispersion Shifted Fibre (ITU-T G.653)
Conventional SMFs has a zero-dispersion wavelength near the 1310 nm band. SMFs
shows high dispersion values over the range between 1500 nm and 1600 nm. Dispersion
shifted ﬁbres (DSFs) have a zero-dispersion value near the 1550 nm wavelength where the
attenuation is minimum. The DSFs are optimized for operating in the region between 1500
to 1600 nm, [11].
2.7.3
Polarisation Maintaining Fibre (PMF)
PMFs are designed birefringent, that is they have a polarization-dependt propagation
constant. Birefringence is presented in subsection 2.2.3. The PMFs have a higher loss than
standard non-PM ﬁbres, [12].
There are two methods of impacting birefringence to an optical ﬁbre. One is using an
elliptical core rather than a circular shape for a step-index ﬁbre. The other is by stress
birefringence, which is obtained by straining the glass in the core so the refractive index
of the core becomes polarization dependent. At the present stress-induced birefringence
seems to be preferable, [12].
Stress-induced birefringence is obtained by surrounding the core with stress elements
that have a diﬀerent thermal expansion coeﬃcient as compared to the core and cladding.
The most common designs for the stress elements are presented in ﬁgure 2.13. The most
common design is the PANDA, as seen to the left in ﬁgure 2.13.
14
==PAGE:25==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
Figure 2.13: Fibre designs for stress-induced core birefringence; PANDA ﬁbre
(left), bowtie ﬁbre (middel) and elliptical-cladding ﬁbre (right). Figure from [12].
For more optical ﬁbre descriptions see appendix A section A.6.
2.8
Optical Network Components
In this section the optical components needed for fault location in submarine power cables
are presented.
2.8.1
Transmitters
The optical transmitters convert an electrical signal into the corresponding optical signal
and launch it into the optical ﬁbre. The major component of optical transmitters is
the optical source. In ﬁbre-optical communication systems the optical source of choice is
semiconductors as, light-emitting diodes, (LEDs) and semiconductor lasers. The advantages
of the semiconductor optical sources include compact size, high eﬃciency, good reliability,
the right wavelength range and the possibility of direct modulation at relatively high
frequencies. For more information about optical transmitters and semiconductors see
section A.7.
Signal-to-Noise Ratio
The two fundamental noise mechanisms for a semiconductor laser are spontaneous emission
and electron-hole recombination (shot noise). The most dominant noise in semiconductors
is spontaneous emission. This is due to the relatively large value of Rsp, as discussed in
subsection A.7.16. This results in ﬂuctuations in intensity and in phase of the emitted
light over time. Intensity ﬂuctuations will lead to a limited signal-to-noise ration (SNR)
and phase ﬂuctuations will lead to a ﬁnite spectral linewidth when the semiconductor laser
is operated at a constant current.
The SNR is deﬁned as ¯p/σp, [3] [6], where σp is the root-mean-square (RMS) noise and
¯p = ⟨p⟩is the average number of photons. For power levels higher than a few milliwatts
the SNR will exceed 20 dB and improves linearly with the power as [3]
SNR =
 ϵNL
Rspτp
1/2
¯p
(2.22)
where ϵNL is the nonlinear-gain parameter and thus indicates the inﬂuences of the nonlinear
form of the gain. Typical values of ϵNL are ≈10−7. This form of the SNR needs to be
15
==PAGE:26==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
modiﬁed for high powers. The SNR eventually saturates at a value of about 30 dB and
becomes power-independent, [3].
Spectral Linewidth
The spectrum of the emitted light from a laser is related to the ﬁeld-autocorrelation
function ΓEE(τ) through a Fourier transformation as, [3]
S(ω) =
Z ∞
−∞
ΓEE(t)exp[−i(ω −ω0)τ]dτ
(2.23)
The integral can be performed and the spectrum is found to be Lorentzian, [3]. The
linewidth is deﬁned as the full-width half-maximum (FWHM) of the spectrum from the
laser and is given by
∆ν = Rsp

c
4π¯p
(2.24)
where βc is the linewidth enhancement factor. From equation 2.24 and equation A.114 it
is seen that the spectral linewidth decreases as ¯p−1 with an increase in the laser power.
The linewidth can also be reduced by increasing the length of the cavity L, as Rsp decreases
and p increases at a given output power when L is increased. When the length dependence
of Rsp and p is incorporated in equation 2.24 it can be shown that ∆ν varies as L−2.
Pulsed Laser
The most direct way to achieve a pulsed laser is to use a continuous wave (CV) laser
together with an external switch or modulator that only allows transmission of light during
selected time intervals. This scheme is presented in ﬁgure 2.14.
Figure 2.14: Pulsed laser output achieved with an external modulator. Figure
from [6].
The external modulator scheme as seen in ﬁgure 2.14 is ineﬃcient as it blocks, and thus
wastes the light, during the oﬀ-time of the pulse train. Furthermore the peak power of the
pulse cannot exceed the steady state power of the cw source, as seen in ﬁgure 2.14.
16
==PAGE:27==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
A more eﬃcient pulsing scheme is based on turning the laser on and oﬀby an internal
modulation process, where the energy can be stored during the oﬀ-time and released again
during the on-time. This scheme is presented in ﬁgure 2.15.
Figure 2.15: Pulsed laser output achieved with an internal modulator. Figure
from [6].
The energy may be stored in the resonator, in the form of light that periodically can escape,
or in the atomic system, in the form of a population inversion which is periodically released
and thus allows the system to oscillate. Such an internal modulation scheme allows for
shorter lasers pulses with peak powers that can exceed the constant power deliverable by
the cw laser.
In the following three diﬀerent methods of internal modulation are presented and mode
locking is presented in subsection A.7.18.
Gain Switching
In the gain swithching approach to internal modulation the gain is controlled by turning
the laser pump on and oﬀ, as seen in ﬁgure 2.16.
Figure 2.16: Gain switching. Figure from [6].
Turning the pumping rate R on and oﬀis equivalent to modulating the small-signal
population diﬀerence, N0 = 2Rtsp −Na, where tsp is the stimulated emission time and Na
is the total atomic density.
A typical time evolution of the population diﬀerence N(t) and the photon-number density
n(t), as the laser is pulsed by varying N0 is presented in ﬁgure 2.17.
At t < 0 the population diﬀerence N(t) = N0a < Nt, is below the threshold Nt and no
oscillation can occur.
For t = 0, the pump is turned on and N0 increases from N0a < Nt, below the threshold
17
==PAGE:28==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
value, to N0b > Nt above the threshold value. The increase happens as a step function.
The population diﬀerence N(t) begins to increase. As long as N(t) < Nt the photon-
number density will remain zero, n = 0.
In this region dN/dT = (N0 −N)/tsp, which indicates that N(t) grows exponentially
toward its equilibrium value N0b with time constant tsp.
Once N(t) ≤Nt at t = t1, the laser oscillation begins and n(t) increases. This in turn
makes the depletion more eﬃcient so that N(t) begins to decay to Nt. When N(t) reaches
Nt, n(t) reaches its steady state.
At t = t2 the pump is turned oﬀ, which reduced N0 to its original value N0a. N(t) decays
to N0a and n(t) decays to 0.
Figure 2.17: Variation of the population diﬀerence N(t) and the photon-number
density n(t) with time, as a square pump results in N0 suddenly increasing from a
low value N0a to a high value N0b and then decreasing back to a low value N0a.
Figure from [6].
Q-Switching
In Q-switching, the laser output is changed by altering the resonator loss αr periodically,
thus altering the resonator quality factor Q, with the help of an absorber inside the
resonator, as seen in ﬁgure 2.18.
Figure 2.18: Q-switching, ﬁgure from [6].
Q-switching is loss switching. When the laser is turned oﬀthe resonator loss coeﬃcient αr
is large and when the laser is turned on, the resonator loss coeﬃcient αr is small. As the
lasing threshold population diﬀerence Nt is proportional to the resonator loss coeﬃcient
18
==PAGE:29==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
αr, the result of switching αr is to decrease Nt from a high value Nta to a lower value Ntb,
as is seen in ﬁgure 2.19
Figure 2.19: Operation of a Q-switched laser.
Variation of the population
threshold Nt (which is proportional to the resonator loss), the pump parameter
N0, the population diﬀerence N(t) and the photon number n(t). Figure from [6].
At t = 0, the pump is turned on and N0 increases in a step function. The loss is kept at a
level suﬃciently high, Nt = Nta > N0, so no laser oscillation can occur. The population
diﬀerence N(t) builds up with a time constant tsp.
At t = t1 the loss Nt suddenly decreases to a value Nt = Ntb < N0 and the oscillation
can begin, and thus the photon-number density n(t) increases rapidly. When N(t) < Ntb
the loss is bigger than the gain and this results in a rapid decrease in the photon-number
density n(t).
At t = t2 the loss is increased so that Nt = Nta and no oscillation can occur. The population
inversion is build up to prepare for the next pulse at t = ti.
Cavity Dumping
In cavity dumping the photons are stored in the resonator during the oﬀ-times, and released
during the on-times.
Cavity dumping diﬀers from Q-switching in the way the the resonator loss is modulated
by alternating the mirror transmittance, as seen in ﬁgure 2.20.
Figure 2.20: Cavity dumping. One of the mirrors is removed altogether to dump
the stored photons as useful light. Figure from [6].
An analogue to the cavity dumping technique is a bucket of water, into which water is
19
==PAGE:30==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
poured from a hose at a constant rate. After a period of time of accumulating water, the
bottom of the bucket is removed and the water is dumped. The bottom is again returned
and the process is repeated. A constant ﬂow of water is in this way converted to a pulsed
ﬂow. For the cavity-dumped laser, the bucket represents the resonator, the water hose
represents the constant pump and the bucket bottom represents the laser output mirror.
Leakage of light from the resonator is not permitted during the oﬀ-times. This results
in a negligible resonator loss and thereby increases the optical power inside the laser
resonator. The photons are stored in the resonator and cannot escape The mirror is
suddenly removed altogether, by increasing its transmittance to 100% during the on-times.
As the accumulated photons leave the resonator, the increase in loss stops the oscillation.
The result is a strong pulse of laser light.
2.8.2
Receivers
The optical receiver does the opposite job as compared to the optical transmitter. The
optical receiver converts the optical signal into an electrical signal and recovers the data
transmitted through the optical lightwave system. The major component of the optical
receiver is the photodetector that converts light into electricity through the photoelectric
eﬀect. The basic concept behind the photodetection process is optical absorption, which is
discussed in subsection A.7.5.
Responsivity and Quantum Eﬃciency
In ﬁgure 2.21 a semiconductor slab used as a photodetector is schematically presented.
Figure 2.21: Schematical representation of a semiconductor slab used as a
photodetector. Figure from [3].
If the energy of the incident photons hν, on the semiconductor slab photodetector, exceeds
the bandgap energy, then an electron-hole pair is generated each time a photon is absorbed
by the semiconductor. With the inﬂuence of an electric ﬁeld, set up by an applied voltage,
the electron and holes are moved across the semiconductor, resulting in a ﬂowing electrical
current.
This photocurrent Ip is directly proportional to the incident optical power Pin, which can
be expressed as Ip = RdPin, where Rd is the responsivity of the photodetector. At larger
values of Rd more current is produced at a given input power.
The responsivity can be expressed in terms of the quantum eﬃciency for the receiver,
20
==PAGE:31==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
which is similar to the quantum eﬃciency for the transmitter as presented in equation
A.112 through A.121, as Rd = (ηq)/(hν) ≈(ηλ)/(1.24), where the quantum eﬃciency is
given as
η = electron generation rate
photon incidence rate
=
Ip/q
Pin/hν = hν
q Rd
(2.25)
with λ = c/ν expressed in micrometers.
The responsivity is linear proportional to the wavelength simply because more photons are
present for the same optical power. This linear relationship continues until the photon
energy becomes to small to generate electrons. For semiconductors this happens when
hν < Eg and the quantum eﬃciency then drops to zero.
The dependency of η on λ enters through the absorption coeﬃcient α.
If the facets of the semiconductor, presented in ﬁgure 2.21, have an antireﬂection coating,
as presented in ﬁgure 2.22, the power transmitted through the slab can be calculated using
the general change in power over a distance, which is limited by absorption α, as presented
in subsection A.8.1.
Figure 2.22: Power through a semiconductor slab used a a photodetector of
width W, with antireﬂection coating and placed in vacuum. Figure from [4].
For the case of the semiconductor slab of width W the transmitted power can be expressed
as
Ptr = Pine−αW
(2.26)
Furthermore the power absorbed by the photodetector is Pabs = Ptr−Pin and with equation
2.26 the transmitted power can be rewritten as
Pabs = Pin[1 −e−αW ]
(2.27)
As each absorbed photon creates an electron-hole pair, the quantum eﬃciency η can be
expressed in terms of the absorbed power,
η = electron generation rate

= 1 −e−αW
(2.28)
As expected, η = 0 for α = 0 and η approaches 1 for αW approaching ∞.

==PAGE:32==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
Figure 2.23 shows the wavelength dependence of α for a number of semiconductors. As
mentioned above, absorption requires hν > Eg, where ν = c/λ, c is the speed of light and
λ is the wavelength, both in vacuum. By substitution of ν with c/λ the it is obtained
that λ < (hc)/Eg, which means that λ must be smaller than an upper limit in order for
absorption to be possible. The upper limit is the absorption edge and it is seen in ﬁgure
2.23.
Figure 2.23:
Absorption coeﬃcient α versus λ for diﬀerent semiconductor
materials. Figure from [13].
Rise Time and Bandwidth
The bandwidth of a photodetector is determined by the speed with which it responds to
variations in the incident optical power, [3].
The rise time Tr is deﬁned as the time over which the current builds up from 10% to 90%
of its ﬁnal value, when the incident optical power is changed abruptly, [3]. The rise time
depends on the time it takes the electrons and holes to travel to the electrical contacts. It
also depends on the response time of the electrical circuit used to process the photocurrent.
The rise time is given as, [3]
Tr = (ln9)(τtr + τRC)
(2.29)
where τtr is the transit time and τRC is the time constant of an equivalent RC circuit. The
transit time is added to τRC as it takes time before for the carriers are collected after their
generation through the absorption of photons. The maximum collection time is equal to
the time it takes an electron to transverse the absorption region.
The transit time τtr can be reduced by decreasing the width of the absorption region
W, but, as seen in equation 2.28, there is a trade-oﬀbetween the bandwidth and the
responsivity, as the speed versus sensitivity, of a photodetector.
The RC time constant τRC often limits the bandwidth due to electrical parasitics. An
example of an electrical parasitics is that a resistor is designed to possess resistance, but
22
==PAGE:33==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
will also possess unwanted parasitic capacitance.
The bandwidth of a photodetector can be expressed as
∆f =
1
2π(τtr + τRC)
(2.30)
Besides the rise time and bandwidth, dark current Id is also an important parameter of
the photodetector. The dark current is generated in a photodetector in the absence of any
optical signal and originates from stray light or from thermally generated electron-hole
pairs. Dark current should be negligible in the order of Id < 10 nA.
Receiver Noise
Even though the photocurrent equation for Ip assumes a noise free conversion from optical
power to electric current, it is not the case, not even for a perfect receiver. Two fundamental
noise mechanisms, shot noise and thermal noise, lead to ﬂuctuation in the current from a
photodetector, even with a constant incident power. The relation from the photocurrent
equation still holds, as long as Ip is the average current.
Shot Noise
Shot noise is the outcome of the fact that an electric current consists of a stream of
electrons that are generated at random times, [3].
The current, a photodiode generates, as a response to a constant optical signal can be
expressed as I(t) = Ip + is, where Ip is from the photocurrent and is is the current
ﬂuctuations related to shot noise.
The noise variance is given by σ2
s = ⟨i2
s(t)⟩= 2qIp∆f, where ∆f is the eﬀective noise
bandwidth of the receiver.
As dark current also generates shot noise, its contribution is included, and the total shot
noise is given as σ2
s = 2q(Ip + Id)∆f, where σs is the root-mean-square value of the noise
current induced by shot noise.
Thermal Noise
Thermal noise is the noise produced due to the thermal motion of electrons in a resistor,
which manifests itself as ﬂuctuating current, even in the absence of an applied voltage, [3].
Thermal noise can be included in the equation for the photocurrent with the shot noise as
I(t) = Ip + is(t) + iT (t), with iT as the current ﬂuctuation induced by thermal noise.
The noise variance is given as σ2
T = ⟨i2
T (t)⟩= /4kBT)/(RL)∆f.
Here the same bandwidth appears in the case of shot noise and thermal noise. The noise
generated in the load resistor is also included.
For an actual receiver, many other electrical components may add additional noise.
Electrical ampliﬁers add additional noise and the ampliﬁer noise can be introduced
by the ampliﬁer noise ﬁgure Fn and thereby modifying the noise variance to σ2
T =
(4kBT)/(RL)Fn∆f.
23

ﬂuctuations can be obtained by adding the individual variances as
σ2 = σ2

APD Receivers
Avalanche photodiode (APD) receivers are described in subsection A.8.4. The APD re-
ceivers usually have a higher SNR, at the same input power, due to the internal gain.
The internal gain increases the photocurrent by a multiplication factor M so that the
photocurrent is Ip = MRdPin = RAPDPin, where RAPD ≡MRd is the responsivity of the
APD, which is enhanced by a factor of M as compared to the p −i −n photodiode. The
SNR of the APD should improve by a factor M2, but this is not the case as the receiver
noise is aﬀected by the internal gain.
Due to this multiplication factor the shot noise increases, whereas the thermal noise stays
the same for the APD receiver, because it originates in the electrical components that are
not a part of the APD. The gain in the APD results from the process of impact ionization,
that generates secondary electron-hole pairs. Since the pairs are generated at random
times, additional contribution to the shot noise is added, associated with the generation
of primary electron-hole pairs. The multiplication factor is itself a random variable and
the M in the photocurrent equation for the APD receivers represents the average APD gain.
The total shot noise for the APD is σ2
s = 2qM2FA(RdPin + Id)∆f, with FA as the excess
noise factor of the APD and it is given as FA(M) = kAM + (1 −kA)(2 −1/M). The
parameter kA = αh/αe for αh < αe and kA = αe/αh for αh > αe, where αe and αh are the
impact ionization coeﬃcients for electrons and holes, respectively, and they are presented
in subsection A.8.4.
This means that kA is in the range 0 < kA < 1. The ratio kA should be as small as possible
in order to achieve the best performance from an APD, [3].
In the shot noise limit the SNR of the APD receiver is worse than for the p−i−n receivers,
due to the excess noise generated inside the APD. It is the dominance in thermal noise in
receivers that makes the APD attractive. The SNR of the APD can be expressed as
SNR =
I2
p
σ2s + σ2
T
=
(MRdPin)2
2qM2FA(RdPin + Id)∆f + 4(kBT/RL)Fn∆f
(2.36)
In ﬁgure 2.24 the dependence of SNR on received power Pin for three values of APD gain
M using Rd = 1 A/W, Id = 1 nA, kA = 0.7 and σT = 1µA for a receiver with a 30-GHz
bandwidth is presented.
For the case of M = 1, which corresponds to a p −i −n photodiode, it is seen, from ﬁgure
2.24, that the SNR is degraded for an APD receiver when input powers are relatively large.
Improvement for the SNR only occurs for low input power levels below -20 dBm. This
behaviour is related to the enhancement of shot noise in APD receivers.
In the low power levels the thermal noise dominated over the shot noise and the APD gain
helps. But as the APD gain increases, the shot noise begins to dominate over the thermal
noise and the performance of the APD receiver is worse than a p −i −n photodiode under
the same operating conditions.

gain M for a receiver with a bandwidth of 30 GHz. The M = 1 case corresponds
to a p −i −n photodiode. Figure from [3].

2.9
Loss Management
The loss in ﬁbres must be compensated for, in long lightwave systems, as their commutative
eﬀect eventually will make the signal so weak, that the receiver cannot detect it.
An ampliﬁer increases the signal, and the noise, and can therefore compensate for the ﬁbre
loss.
Two ampliﬁcation schemes are presented; lumped and distributed.
The lumped ampliﬁcation scheme is used in for example Erbium-Doped Fibre Ampliﬁers
(EDFAs) where the ampliﬁers are used periodically along a ﬁbre link, as presented in ﬁgure
2.25.
The length of each erbium-doped ﬁbre ampliﬁer, la, is typically much shorter than the
spacing, LA between two ampliﬁers. The loss of each section reduces the average power
26
==PAGE:37==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
by a factor of exp(αLA), as seen from equation 2.13. The lumped ampliﬁer must operate
such that its gain is GA = exp(g0la) = exp(αLA), in order to compensate for the loss.
The power throughout the ﬁbre link with lumped ampliﬁcation is also presented in ﬁgure
2.25. Here it is seen that the power after the ampliﬁers drops as exp(αLA).
Figure 2.25: Lumped ampliﬁcation, where the ampliﬁers, the triangles, are
placed periodically along the ﬁbre link.LA is the spacing between two ampliﬁers.
The red line on the graph is the optical power through the ﬁbre link with lumped
ampliﬁcation. Figure from [4].
The distributed ampliﬁcation scheme uses the transmission ﬁbre itself for signal
ampliﬁcation by using stimulated Raman scattering.
Raman scattering is presented
in subsection 2.5.3. Raman ampliﬁers require one or more pump lasers, at a suitable
wavelength, which inject optical power periodically, as seen in ﬁgure 2.26.
The gain in a distributed ampliﬁer is dependent on the position along the ﬁbre link, g0(z).
Ideally would g0(z) = α and it would be constant along the ﬁbre link and therefore the
average power would be constant over the ﬁbre link as well. This is not the case in practice,
where the distributed gain is realized by injecting pump power periodically into the ﬁbre
link. As pump power is not constant, due to ﬁbre loss at the pump wavelength, the gain
factor g(z), cannot be kept constant along the ﬁbre. The losses can be compensated fully
over a distance of LA, the pump-station spacing, if the following condition is satisﬁed
Z LA
0
g0(d)dz = αLA
(2.39)
The scheme works by launching continuous power at several wavelengths from the pump-
stations, into the ﬁbre link. The pump power then ampliﬁes the optical signal. The power
variation of a bidirectional distributed ampliﬁcation is presented in ﬁgure 2.26. In the
bidirectional pumping scheme optical power is pumped in both the forward and backward
direction.
27
==PAGE:38==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
Figure 2.26: Distributed ampliﬁcation, where ampliﬁcation requires one or more
pump lasers to inject optical power periodically in the ﬁbre link. LA is the pump-
station spacing. The green line on the graph is the optical power through the ﬁbre
link with bidirectional distributed ampliﬁcation. Figure from [4].
2.9.1
Erbium-Doped Fibre Ampliﬁers (EDFA)
A description of the mechanisms in EDFAs is presented in subsection A.9.1.
Ampliﬁer Noise
When the signal in an optical ﬁbre link is ampliﬁed, the noise will be ampliﬁed as well. In
ﬁgure 2.27 a block diagram of an optical ampliﬁer is presented. The signal power is Ps,
the gain from the ampliﬁer is G and the ampliﬁed signal power is GPs. Furthermore the
noise is ampliﬁed at the + in ﬁgure 2.27.
Figure 2.27: Block diagram of an optical ampliﬁer. Figure from [13].
The noise is added due to ampliﬁed spontaneous emission (ASE),[13] and it has a spectral
density of Ssp = 2(G −1)nsphν, where nsp is the spontaneous emission factor given as
nsp = N2/(N2 −N1), where N1 and N2 are the population in the ground and excited state,
respectively.
28
==PAGE:39==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
In the optical gain bandwidth ∆ν, the emitted noise power is Psp = Ssp∆ν = 2(G −
1)nsphν∆ν, where Ssp is assumed constant over the optical bandwidth, because ∆ν << ν,
[13].
Normally ∆ν is much larger than the bandwidth of the signal, therefore an optical bandpass
ﬁlter with a smaller bandwidth ∆νopt is often placed behind the ampliﬁer in order to
reduce the noise power to Psp = Ssp∆νopt = 2(G −1)nsphν∆νopt.
In ﬁgure 2.28 the gain bandwidth of the optical ampliﬁer in relation to the optical bandpass
ﬁler with is bandwidth ∆νopt is presented, the much more narrow spectrum of the optical
signal is also shown. The center frequency of the ﬁlter is placed on the center frequency ν0
of the optical signal.
Figure 2.28:
Optical ﬁlter characteristic, spectrum of signal and optical
ampliﬁcation versus frequency.
The optical ﬁlter removes much of the noise
power, but passes the signal spectrum. Figure from [13].

2.10
Optical Time Domain Reﬂectometer (OTDR)
When measuring the loss in an optical ﬁbre, often the total loss is suﬃcient information.
This is usually measured by injecting an optical power Pin of for example a continuous
wave (cw) laser into the ﬁbre under test. At the output of the ﬁbre a power meter or an
Optical Spectrum Analyser (OSA) is used to measure the output power Pout and the loss
Lfibre can be calculated as Lfibre[dB] = Pin[dBm] −Pout[dBm]. But in some cases the
loss may be too high, and information about where the loss is too high and what may have
caused the high loss, is needed. In the extreme case of a ﬁbre break it will result in no
output power from the ﬁbre, and without any information the break can potentially be
anywhere on the ﬁbre. This is illustrated in ﬁgure 2.29.
Figure 2.29: Measuring the total power of a ﬁbre under test, including a break.
Figure from [5].
There is therefore a need for a tool to measure how the loss in the optical ﬁbre is distributed.
Such a tool can be an Optical Time Domain Reﬂectometer (OTDR) and the principle of
the equipment is seen ﬁgure 2.30
Figure 2.30: Principle of measuring the loss properties of a ﬁbre under test with
an OTDR. Figure from [5].
31
==PAGE:42==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
As seen in ﬁgure 2.30 the box labelled OTDR consists of several components. In the
box there is a laser which is controlled by the signal processing unit. The laser will emit
an optical square-shaped pulse with temporal width τ. The pulse is injected into the
optical ﬁbre under test through a coupler. Due to Rayleigh scattering, as discussed in
subsection 2.5.1, information about splicing, connectors, bending and a potential break
will be reﬂected back to the OTDR box. The reﬂected signal is converted to the electrical
domain, sampled and is applied to the signal processing unit. The signal processing unit
will then generate a plot of the loss in the ﬁbre as a function of distance, as seen in ﬁgure
2.30.
As the signal is propagating though the ﬁbre, is Rayleigh scattered and has to propagate
back to the input of the ﬁbre, a speciﬁc time delay between the launch time and the
arrival of the Rayleigh backscattered light will occur. The total distance for propagating
to position L and back again is 2L. The speed of the light in the material is dependent
on the speed of light in vacuum and on the refractive index of the ﬁbre, as v = c
n. The
speciﬁc time delay will be ∆T(L) = (2L)/(c/n).
By using the equation for the power of the reﬂected signal, as in equation 2.16, sampling
the reﬂected signal and keeping track of the time between the launched signal and the
temporal sampling, the reﬂected signal from position L can be found. This is seen in ﬁgure
2.31, where the OTDR trace is presented as a function of time. By using the equation
for the speciﬁc time delay of ∆T(L), this can be converted into a trace as a function of
distance, as seen in ﬁgure 2.30.
Figure 2.31: Illustration of OTDR trace as a function of distance in a ﬁbre
under test, ﬁgure from [5].
As seen in ﬁgure 2.30 and 2.31 the ﬁbre under test may have positions where the loss has
an abrupt change due to for example splicing or bend of the ﬁbre.
The back reﬂectance is formally deﬁned by the power of the reﬂectance, R, that being the
fraction of incident power that is reﬂected from the interface. For a boundary between two
materials, as ﬁbre and air in the case of a break, with refractive indices n1 and n2, the
power reﬂectance is
R =
n1 −n2
n1 + n2
2
(2.48)
32
==PAGE:43==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
When the incident light to the boundary is perpendicular, equation 2.48 is fulﬁlled in
ﬁbres. In the case of ﬁbre made from glass with refractive index of n1 = 1.46 and air with
n2 = 1.00 the reﬂectance is R = 0.03, which in logarithmic scale is R = −14.56 dB and
the ﬁbre break will manifest itself as a reﬂection without any Rayleigh scattering after its
position.
Thus the loss and reﬂections can be determined by the use of the OTDR principle.
2.10.1
Dead Zones
The dead zone of an OTDR refers to the distance or time where the OTDR cannot detect
or localize an event in the optical ﬁbre under test, [16]. The dead zone is caused by Fresnel
reﬂection, which comes from an air gap in the OTDR connection to the optical ﬁbre, and
the subsequent recovery time of the OTDR detector, [17]. Dead zones are always present
at the very beginning of a OTDR trace and at any other high reﬂectance event. When a
high reﬂectance occur, the photodiode inside of the OTDR becomes saturated with the
power received from the reﬂected light, which can be more than 4,000 times higher than
that of the backscattered power, [16] [17]. Therefore the OTDR needs time to recover
from its saturated condition. During this recovering time the OTDR can not detect the
backscattered signal accurately and this will result in the corresponding dead zone seen on
the OTDR trace, as presented in ﬁgure 2.32.
Figure 2.32: Illustration of OTDR trace, with power as a function of distance.
The dead zone in the OTDR connection to the optical ﬁbre is indicated with red
font. Figure from [16].
An analogy to the dead zone is when your eyes have to recover from looking into a bright
ﬂash of a camera. In general, the higher the reﬂectance, the longer the dead zone is.
Furthermore the dead zone is inﬂuenced by the pulse width, as a longer pulse width can
increase the dynamic range which can result in a longer dead zone, [18]. Dispersion of the
pulse width could inﬂuence the dead zone of the detection, but this is assumed calibrated
in the fault detection products on the market.
On the OTDR trace there will be two types of dead zones, the attenuation dead zone and
the event dead zone, both are presented in the following.
33
==PAGE:44==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
Attenuation Dead Zone
The attenuation dead zone (ADZ) is the minimum distance after a reﬂection at which a
non-reﬂective event can be detected, [16]. This is the location where the signal is within 0.5
dB deviation from a straight line ﬁt to the backscatter level, presented in ﬁgure 2.33. The
backscatter level is the slope from the line on the trace which provides the ﬁbre attenuation
value.
The purpose of the ADZ is to give an indication of the distance after a connector at which
an accurate loss measurement can be measured. In order to compensate for the ADZ,
when measuring a short ﬁbre or a patch cord, a longer optical ﬁbre can be placed in front
of the patch cord under test.
Figure 2.33: Attenuation dead zone deﬁnition. Figure from [17].
Event Dead Zone
The event dead zone (EDZ) is the distance between two cursors set at 1.5 dB below the
reﬂective peak, presented in ﬁgure 2.34.
The purpose of the EDZ is to give an indication of the distance after a connector at which
an accurate length measurement can be performed. As for the ADZ the EDZ can be
compensated for by placing a long ﬁbre in front of a shorter ﬁbre under test.
Figure 2.34: Event dead zone deﬁnition. Figure from [17].
2.10.2
Sampling Resolution
The sampling resolution of an OTDR is deﬁned as the minimum distance between two
consecutive sampling points acquired by the instrument, [19]. The error generated by the
34
==PAGE:45==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
sampling resolution is a ﬁxed value, but the maximum number of acquisitions points for
a given distance range for the OTDR must also be taken into account. In ﬁgure 2.35 an
example of sampling error is presented.
Figure 2.35: Example of sampling resolution error. Figure from [19].
A maximum number of acquisition points for an OTDR is usually 128,000, [19]. If a 10
km ﬁbre is under test and the OTDR is set to measure in a range of 10 km, the OTDR
will generate an error of 10,000,000/128,000 ≈± 8 cm, as presented in ﬁgure 2.35.
With a 0.5 km ﬁbre under test, the OTDR will generate an error of 500,000/128,000 ≈±
4 cm, as presented in ﬁgure 2.35.
Figure 2.35, and the calculations presented above, present that a component, such as a
splice, which is not located at an acquisition point will result in an error of ± sampling
resolution.
Using the highest sampling resolution, meaning the smallest sampling value, will increase
the acquisition time, [19]. The acquisition time is proportional to the sampling resolution
setting, when aiming to reach the same dynamic range.
This can be seen in the fact, that the acquisition time to make a trace with a 0.5 meter
sampling resolution is approximately four times longer, than making the same trace with a
sampling resolution of 2 meters.
Furthermore it is critical to optimize the distance measurement range to be as close to the
ﬁbre length as possible. This is critical because the acquisition time is proportional to the
change in distance measurement range.
If the same sampling resolution is used and the same dynamic range is experted, the
acquisition time for testing a 2 km ﬁbre with a 8 km distance range is 4 times longer, than
testing the same ﬁbre with a 2 km distance resolution, [19].
2.10.3
Coherent-OTDR (C-OTDR)
A C-OTDR is an instrument used to measure loss and reﬂections in an optically ampliﬁed
system, [20]. As compared to a regular OTDR, described in section 2.10, which launches and
measures the backscatter in a single ﬁbre, the C-OTDR uses a ﬁbre pair for the operation.
Furthermore optical ampliﬁers, as EFDA’s, only amplify in the forward direction and
therefore the backscattered light is not able to return via its original path in the ﬁbre.
With the use of an optical feedback path with in the EDFA, this feedback allows for the
backscattered light to return to the C-OTDR in the in-coming ﬁbre.
A schematic illustration of OTDR with repeaters and C-OTDR with repeaters is seen in
ﬁgure 2.36.
35
==PAGE:46==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
Figure 2.36: Schematic illustration of OTDR with repeaters and C-OTDR with
repeaters. The bold lines represent the backscatter. It is seen that OTDR can
only measure backscatter up to the ﬁrst repeater, where C-OTDR can cross the
repeaters by the use of two optical ﬁbres. Figure from [20].
For the C-OTDR a test signal is launched into the outgoing ﬁbre and the backscattered
signal (the bold lines in ﬁgure 2.36) is received in the in-coming ﬁbre, as seen in ﬁgure 2.36.
The light scattered in the transmission ﬁbre is coupled to the incoming ﬁbre in the high
loss loop back couplers (HLLB in ﬁgure 2.36) placed in each ampliﬁer pair in the repeaters.
2.11
Temperature and Strain Sensing
Temperature and strain can be measured with optical ﬁbres, by several methods. In this
section two methods; by Brillouin scattering and Raman scattering, are presented.
Strain is the ratio of the extension to an original length, [21]. It can be described by the
following equation
ϵ = stran = extension
length
= ∆L
L
(2.49)
Strain is unitless, as it is a ratio of length measurements, [22].
2.11.1
Brillouin Scattering
As seen in ﬁgure 2.5 and discussed in subsection 2.5.2, Brillouin scattering generates a
Stokes (frequency down-shifted) component and an Anti-Stokes (frequency up-shifted)
component. By measuring the diﬀerence in the Brillouin shift, meaning the frequency shift
of the Anti-Stokes component, the temperature and strain can be measured.
The Brillouin frequency shift increases linearly with strain and temperature, which is
presented in [23] as vB(ϵ) = vB(0)[1 + Csϵ] and vB(t) = vB(tr)[1 + Ct(t −tr)], respectively.
Here ϵ is the tensile strain, t is the temperature and tr as the reference temperature.
36
==PAGE:47==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
The proportional coeﬃcients of strain and temperature are found to be Cs = 4.6 and
Ct = 9.4 × 10−5 K, respectively, [23]. It should be noted that the strain in the equation
νB(ϵ) is the strain in the longitudinal direction induced along the length of the ﬁbre. Lat-
eral pressure strain up to 2.2 kg/m has shown to have little eﬀect on the Brillouin shift, [23].
Two possible methods to detect the Brillouin backscatter are displayed in ﬁgure 2.37.
Figure 2.37: Two methods for detecting Brillouin backscatter; (a) Coherent
detection method, (b) Counterpropagating wave method. Figure from [23].
In ﬁgure 2.37 (a) the coherent detection method also known as B-OTDR is schematically
presented. Here a pulsed laser is launched into a ﬁbre and the backward spontaneous
Brillouin scattering is measured with a coherent detection receiver by mixing it with a
local laser.
Figure 2.37 (b) schematically shows the counterpropagating wave method also know as
Brillouin-Optical Time Domain Analysis (B-OTDA). The method uses Brillouin gain
spectroscopy where a pulsed laser and a counterpropagating cw laser are launched into
a ﬁbre. When the frequency diﬀerence ∆v between the pulsed laser and the cw laser is
tuned to be the Brillouin frequency shift vB of the ﬁbre, the cw laser will experience a
gain through the stimulated Brillouin process. The increase in power of the cw laser is
measured as a function of time and can be measured in the same manner as a regular OTDR.
Another method for using Brillouin scattering for ﬁbre sensing is Brillouin distributed
temperature sensing (Brillouin-DTS), as presented in [24]. Here it is seen that the Brillouin
frequency shift increases linearly with temperature as
νB(T) = νB0 + CT (T −T0)
(2.50)
37
==PAGE:48==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
where νB0 is the Brillouin frequency at a reference temperature T0 and CT is determined
by the ﬁbre at use.
In order to determine the location of the change in temperature an ampliﬁcation of the
Brillouin scattering is needed. This can be achieved by a cw pump laser at frequency
νCW interacting with a counter-propagating pulsed light wave at frequency νp within the
ﬁbre, [24]. Here it is seen that the Brillouin power gain is maximised when the Brillouin
frequency shift at a local ﬁbre location is equal to the frequency diﬀerence between the cw
pump and the pulsed waves for the local temperature increase. The maximum Brillouin
power gain can be determined by recording the pulsed laser power over time.
Furthermore the response time of the Brillouin system to change in the ﬁbre temperature
is dependent of the pulse propagation time, the data processing time and the response
time of the ﬁbre core, cladding and jacket. The pulse propagation time is determined by
t = 2nL/c, with t as time.
The data processing time is determined by the electronic response time in the equipment.
The delay time between the launch of the pulsed beam and those regions where Brillouin
scattering is ampliﬁed will provide information about a change in temperature.
In [24] as laser with a wavelength of 1319 nm and an 11 km SMF-28 sensing ﬁbre was used. A
CT value of 1.22 MHz/ ℃is regarded standard for a SMF-28 and by this a linear relationship
between the temperature and frequency is formulated as νB(T) = 12796 + 1.22(T −20)
and seen in ﬁgure 2.38.
Figure 2.38: Temperature-frequency calibration conﬁrms a linear relation between
the two variables. The slope is the value of CT . Figure from [24].
2.11.2
Raman Scattering
The Raman scattering process is described in subsection 2.5.3. The methods for measuring
Raman scattering are similar to measuring Brillouin scattering.
The Anti-Stokes component of Raman scattering is temperature dependent, as indicated
in ﬁgure 2.39. The ratio of the Anti-Stokes and Stokes components will provide the local
temperature measurement.
38
==PAGE:49==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
Figure 2.39: The Raman distributed temperature sensing technique. Figure from
[25].
2.12
Distributed Vibration Sensing (DVS)
Two diﬀerent methods for DVS is presented; polarization-OTDR and phase-sensitive
OTDR.
2.12.1
Polarization-OTDR
For the polarization-OTDR method the target information is encoded in the state of
polarization (SOP) of the Rayleigh backscattered light. The simplest polarization-sensitive
OTDR is build up of an OTDR followed by a linear polarizer and the ﬁbre under test, as
seen in ﬁgure 2.40.
Figure 2.40: Schematic set-up for polarization-OTDR. Figure from [26].
The OTDR launches polarized pulses into the ﬁbre. Rayleigh backscatter occurs along
the propagation. The power of the backscattered light is measured as a function of the
pulse position by the OTDR. Before the backscattered light reaches the OTDR it passes
through the polarizer, where the measured power evolution depends on the backscattered
SOP variation.
The basic principle of using P-OTDR to perform distributed vibration sensing consists of
measuring the P-OTDR trance in a dynamic manner. It is possible to obtain the evolution
with time of the Rayleigh signal backscattered at a speciﬁc position z on the ﬁbre, as
PB(z, t). By the use of a Fourier analysis of PB(z, t) for every z, the frequency spectrum
corresponding to the polarization change of the backscattered light at z, can be determined.
39
==PAGE:50==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
2.12.2
Phase-sensitive OTDR (Φ-OTDR)
In phase-OTDR the phase of the Rayleigh backscattered light is used to measure
vibration. Phase-OTDR conﬁguration is similar to OTDR, but here the laser source
is a narrow linewidth laser, meaning it has a narrow optical spectrum and a low phase
noise. Furthermore the laser has a coherent length much longer than the ﬁbre length,
meaning that it is close to a perfect sinusoidal wave.
For a given section of the ﬁbre, the phase-diﬀerence between the backscattered light from
the two ends of a given section , ∆φ, is a function of the length of that section of ﬁbre, L,
as seen in ﬁgure 2.41.
Figure 2.41: Principle of a dynamic strain sensor using Φ-OTDR. Represen-
tation of the behaviour of two groups of scatterers inside the sensing ﬁbre before
and after external perturbation. Figure from [27].
In the unperturbed or undisturbed sections of the ﬁbre under test the length and the
phase-diﬀerence remain unchanged. But if a perturbation which includes a strain, ϵ, occurs
on any section of the ﬁbre, this will alter the phase diﬀerence. The phase-diﬀerence is given
by, ∆φ = ϵL(β −(1/2)βn2[(1 −µ)p12 −µp11]) [27], with β as the propagation constant, n
as the refractive index, µ as Poisson’s ration, which is the ratio of transverse stain to axial
strain, and p11 and p12 are the stain-optic coeﬃcients.
By measuring the diﬀerence in phase of the backscattered light from any two sections in
the ﬁbre under test, the stain variations of that section can be found.
The attenuation for a SMF is 0.2 dB/km and will therefore be 0.4 dB/km, as the signal
has to travel out and back again in the ﬁbre. The input signal has a maximum power limit,
otherwise non-linear eﬀects will occur, this, together with the attenuation of the ﬁbre, will
limit the distance range of the phase-OTDR to 40-50 km, [28].
40
==PAGE:51==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
The measuring techniques for the Φ-OTDR can be divided into three categories;
• Duel-pulse Technique
In this technique a pair of temporally separated pulses are used to investigate the
ﬁbre, as seen in ﬁgure 2.42. With the temporal separation of ∆T, this will results
in the backscattered light from the ﬁrst pulse at position x1 to be mixed with the
backscattered light from the second pulse at position x2. The spatial separation
between the two locations is given by, ∆X = ∆T · c/2n.
Figure 2.42: Experiment setup for DVS with dual-pulse technique. Figure from
[27].
An acousto-optic modulation (AOM in ﬁgure 2.42) is used to simultaneously modulate
the cw laser to form the pulses and shift their frequencies. The signal from the two
backscattered traces from the two pulses will result in a signal at the photodetector
with a beat frequency of ∆f = |f1 −f2|.
• Interferometry Technique
In this technique the relative phase of the backscattered light between two separate
sections is measured by the use of a Mach-Zehnder interferometer, as seen in ﬁgure
2.43.
Figure 2.43: Experiment setup for DVS with interferometry technique. Figure
from [27].
For this technique the backscattered light from a single pulse is sent into the Mach-
Zehnder interferometer, that has two arms of unequal length in order to provide two
traces with the temporal shift of ∆T = ∆L · n/c. Here ∆L is the path diﬀerence
between the two arms of the Mach-Zehnder interferometer. Before the backscattered
light reaches the photodetectors the signals from the two arms are mixed in a 3 × 3
coupler, which provides three waves with a phase-shift of 120 ℃between them and
this avoids that the signals fades.
• Electrical-domain Technique
In this technique the data is converted from the optical domain to the electrical
41
==PAGE:52==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
domain, before the phase diﬀerence between two sections of the ﬁbre is measured.
Here an acousto-optic modulator (AOM in ﬁgure 2.44) is used to generate optical
pulses with a frequency shift of ∆f, relative to the light source.
Figure 2.44: Experiment setup for DVS with electrical-domain technique. Figure
from [27].
The backscattered light from the ﬁbre is mixed with the light source in a photodetector,
in order to generate a beat signal which maintains both the information about the
amplitude and the phase. The information about the phase along the ﬁbre is measured
in the electrical domain by the use of a phase detection circuit.
2.13
Optical Frequency Domain Reﬂectometer (OFDR)
OFDR can be used with all the scattering types presented in section 2.5. The system
provides information about the ﬁbre under test when the backscattered light is measured
as a function of frequency and then is subjected to a Fourier transformation.
The advantages of using OFDR is the narrow band detection of the backscattered signal
which can be achieved by the use of a quasi-cw laser operation, and thereby a higher SNR
can be achieved, as compared to OTDR, [29]. The quasi-cw laser operation is described in
A.7.19.
2.14
Magnetic Field Fibre Sensor Technique
The magnetic ﬁeld strength can be measured by the use of detection of the Faraday rotation
of the optical polarization direction, [30]. The Faraday eﬀect is described in subsection
2.6.1.
In order to measure the Faraday rotation of the angle of the output light, two main
detection schemes can be used, either the polarimetric or the interferometric scheme. Both
utilizing linear polarised continuous laser light.
2.14.1
The Polarimetric Detection Scheme
The polarimetric detection scheme consist of two polarizers, one at the input of the sensor-
part and one at the output, as seen in ﬁgure 2.45, [7], [31]. Here B is the magnetic ﬁeld
strength.
The ﬁrst polarizer deﬁnes the initial polarization of the incident light and the second
polarizer analyses the polarization and transforms the rotation into a light intensity
modulation that can be measured using a photodetector.
42
==PAGE:53==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
Figure 2.45: Polarimetric detection scheme. Figure from [7].
In accordance to Malau’s law of two polarizers, in the alignment presented in ﬁgure 2.45,
the intensity of the output light is given as Iout = IP1cos2θ, with IP1 as the intensity of
the light after the ﬁrst polarizer and θ being the relative angle between the transmission
axes of two polarizers.
From ﬁgure 2.46 it is seen that the maximum output intensity is achieved when the relative
angle between the two polarizers are at 3π/4. This means that when the two polarizers
have a relative angle of 135° or 45°, any small change in the polarization of the light in
the path between the two polarizators, induced by an external magnetic ﬁeld, will be
transformed to a larger change in the intensity at the output.
Figure 2.46: Transfer function of two polarizers showing the intensity of the
output light, Iout as a function of the relative angle, θ. Figure from [7].
With the diﬀerence between the transmission axes of the polarizer and the analyser set to
45°, the intensity of the light detected after propagating through the system is then given
by I = (Iin/2)[1+sin(2θf)], [32], with θf as the angle of rotation as described in subsection
2.6.1 and Iin as the input intensity of the light. In order to eliminate the dependence on
the light intensity, the ﬁnal processed output can be presented as the ratio, S = sin(2θf)
[32].
43
==PAGE:54==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
Dual Quadrature Polarimetric Detection Scheme
An improvement of the polarimetric detection scheme is to divide the output into two
orthogonal linearly polarized beams, through a Wollaston prism, as seen in ﬁgure 2.47. A
Wollaston prism separates randomly polarized or unpolarized light into two orthogonal
linearly polarized outgoing beams, [33]. These two signals are detected separately by two
independent photodetectors and fed into an analogue circuit which computes the output
function, as
S = I1 −I2
I1 + I2
(2.51)
where I1 and I2 are the intensities of the two beams.
Figure 2.47: The dual-quadrature polarimetric detection incorporating optical
ﬁbre links. Here GRIN is gradient index, PD is photodetector and Pi, i = x, y is
the optical power in the polarization states. Figure from [7].
When the Wollaston prism is aligned to make the output ratio S to fall to zero, when
there is no magnetic ﬁeld present, the system can be used to measure the current-induced
Faraday rotation θf, and equation 2.51 becomes as the ratio S = sin(2θf).
When the Faraday rotation is relatively small and the linear birefringence eﬀect in the
sensing element is included, the output signal becomes S = 2θf sinBm
Bm
if Bm ≫2θf or
S = 2θf if Bm ≪2θf, where Bm is the linear birefringence as described in section 2.2.3.
The two polarimetric detection method presented do not take account for the linear
birefringence eﬀect, which can reduce the sensitivity of the sensor. The linear birefringence
is usually temperature dependent and thus measurement errors can be introduced.
2.14.2
The Interferometric Detection Scheme
In the polarimetric detection scheme the rotation of the plane of polarization of linear
polarized light is analyzed.
The rotation can also be analyzed in terms of circular
polarization, which corresponds to a phase diﬀerence between the two circular orthogonal
modes, meaning the left-handed and right-handed circular polarization, [7].
This analyzis can be done by the use of an interferometric detection scheme, in which
a modulation frequency carrier is generated, and the optical phase variation, which
is modulated by the time delay in the arms of the interferometer, will contain the
44
==PAGE:55==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
electrical current information.
The phase carrier can be generated using a Mach-
Zehnder interferometer or an unbalanced Michelson interferometer or other interferometric
conﬁgurations. In ﬁgure 2.48 a Sagnac interferometer is presented for the task.
Figure 2.48: Sagnac loop interferometer current sensor. Figure from [7].
In the set-up seen in ﬁgure 2.48 the light from an optical broadband source is linearly
polarized with a ﬁbre polarizer and is then injected into the Sagnac loop with crossed λ/4
plates placed at an angle of 45° and -45° relative to the plane of polarization of the linearly
polarized inputs, for the upper and lower plate, respectively. In the set-up each of the
counter propagating waves are converted to orthogonal circular states. The two counter
propagating waves that will travel in the Sagnec loop will travel at diﬀerent velocities, due
to the external magnetic ﬁeld induced circular birefringence. The two counter propagating
waves are after the loop converted into linear polarization modes and they interfere after
crossing the output linear polarizer. The phase information is collected by the phase
modulator on the Mach-Zehnder interferometer, which can be used to generate the phase
carrier. In the end the relative phase accumulated in the Sagnac loop can be detected.
The relative phase is proportional to the magnetic ﬁeld generated by the electric current
in the conductor.
Reﬂection Conﬁguration of the Interferometric Detection Scheme
An improvement of the interferometric detection scheme can be made by a reﬂection
conﬁguration, as presented in ﬁgure 2.49
Figure 2.49: In-line interferometer current sensor. Figure from [7].
45
==PAGE:56==
2. Background Info Regarding Optical Fibres
After the light source and before the λ/4 plates, the polarized light is injected at 45° into
a polarization maintaining ﬁbre (PMF). After the PMF the λ/4 plates, which are oriented
at 45° with respect to the birefringence axis of the PMF, transform the two orthogonal
polarization modes into circular polarization modes with opposite direction of rotation.
When the light reaches the end of the ﬁbre reﬂection occurs and the reverse process
takes place. Because of the reﬂection process, the states of polarization swap and each
polarization component travels the optical path made by the other polarization. In the end,
both polarizations pass through the same optical path and compensate all reciprocal eﬀect,
and at the same time maintain a phase diﬀerence proportional to the induced magnetic
ﬁeld. Because of the reﬂection in this set-up scheme it doubles the sensitivity compared to
the interferometric detection scheme, presented in subsection 2.14.2, for the same number
of ﬁbre turns around the conductor.
For the two interferometric detection schemes presented, the time-varying disturbances that
will alter the birefringence of the PMF, aﬀecting the counter-propagating waves diﬀerently
are not compensated for. Furthermore, when working with interferometers, stable optical
sources are a necessity. Lastly the phase diﬀerence introduced by the λ/4 plates can cause
a problem, as they can have small derivations and therefore change the sensibility of the
system.
2.15

presented in table 2.2. Where temp. is short for temperature and vib. is short for vibration.
