3
REVIEW OF THE STATE OF THE ART
In this section a review of the state of the art regarding submarine fault detection is
presented. In order to secure the sources and maintain the correct scientiﬁc practice, all
the locations described in the papers presented are investigated. If the authors of a paper
states that it has been working on a project, the project is investigated in order to ensure
the sincerity of the paper.
3.1
OTDR and C-OTDR
In optical telecommunication great lengths of submarine ﬁbre are already installed and
running. One such ﬁbre is the optic telecommunication cable between the Norwegian
mainland and Svalbard, [34]. The connection consists of two ﬁbre optic cables with subsea
cable lengths of 1339 km and 1375 km.
OTDR and coherent OTDR (C-OTDR) are used for monitoring and fault detection of the
optical submarine cable, as described in subsection 2.10.3.
Each cable has 20 optical repeaters, spaced approximately 67 km apart. Each repeater
has eight optical ampliﬁer pairs, one per ﬁbre pair, and contains a high loss loop back
(HLLB). The repeaters operate on DC power. The ampliﬁer pairs inside the repeaters
each contains two EDFAs and has its own power supply and operates independently of
the other ampliﬁer pairs. A Power Feed Equipment (PEE) provides power to the optical
repeaters and feeds the system with a constant current of 1.1 A at 2500 V.
3.2
Brillouin Scattering used for Fibre Sensing
With the use of coherent detection of the Brillouin backscattering for temperature sensing,
it has been shown possible to make distributed temperature measurements over a 150-km
range, [35], with Raman ampliﬁcation. Here a temperature resolution of 0.8 ℃at a sensing
range of 100 km with a copropagating Raman pump was achieved and a temperature
resolution of 5.2 ℃at a sensing range of 150 km with a 50-m spatial resolution was achieved
with a counterpropagating Raman pump.
Another method for utilising Brillouin scattering to ﬁbre sensing is Brillouin-DTS
(Distributed Temperature Sensing), as presented in [36]. The results from a ﬁeld trial, where
the Brilluin-DTS interrogator monitors the submarine export cable to the Nordsee-Ost
oﬀshore wind farm, shows that for distances up to 50 km, the spatial resolution was of 3
m and the temperature resolution was better than 1 ℃with a measuring time of less than
10 minutes, [36], [37].
48
==PAGE:59==
3. Review of the State of the Art
3.3
Temperature Sensor based on Raman-OFDR
In the 2008 Olympic city of Beijing, a Raman distributed temperature monitoring system
with OFDR was used for monitoring a 7 km long 220 kV power cable, [38]. The technology
used for the project was the LIOS OTS100P, which has a spatial resolution of 1.5 m, a
temperature resolution of 1 K and a measurement time of 3 minutes. The technology can
hold 8 ﬁbre optical channels.
The average temperature of the tunnel, which the cable was placed in, was 20 ℃. The
lowest temperature measurement was 16 ℃and seen in the area of a railway crossing,
where the laying depth was increased. Several increases in the temperature measurement,
where due to the cable crossing high and medium high voltage cables.
Temperature sensing by the use of Raman-OFDR can also be used for even longer ﬁbres
as presented in [39]. Here a LIOS OTS-200P-4CH Raman-OFDR DTS controller was
investigated for sequential monitoring of three submarine power cables of lengths up to
18.3 km. The measuring time was 5 minutes with a resolution of 2 m.
The measurements were performed at the Burbo Bank oﬀshore wind farm, [40]. The
wind farm consists of 25 wind turbines and is located oﬀthe cost of the River Mersey in
Liverpool Bay, Wales, UK. Each power cable is connected to up to 9 wind turbines. The
optical sensing ﬁbres are incorporated in the shielding of the power cables.
3.4
Polarisation-sensitive OTDR
The Xingeng Line is a 500 kV high-voltage power transmission line of 48 km, located in
Shanxi Province, China. Here a system of disturbance detection by the use of polarization-
sensitive optical time domain reﬂectometry (P-OTDR) was implemented, [41].
A ﬁeld trail at the Xingeng Line of a 10-channel system with a sensing distance of around
50 km for each channel was performed. In total 108 cables with range from 10 to 50 km
were investigated, where three point disturbance events at 3.06 km, 8.18 km and 10.49
km were detected. The proposed method has been compared to the traditional direct
detection method as well as the frequency spectra analysis method and it allegedly has the
best multi-point detection and pinpointing performance, [41].
Polarization-OTDR can also be used for distributed optical ﬁbre vibration sensing, as
demonstrated in [42]. Here a spectrum analysis of polarization-OTDR was used to analyse
vibrational disturbances up to 5 kHz in a 1 km ﬁbre link and a spatial resolution of 10
meters is demonstrated.
3.5
Phase-sensitive OTDR
By the use of Raman optical ampliﬁcation, signal detection along the whole sensing length
of a ﬁbre of 62 km was demonstrated by the use of phase-sensitive OTDR (Φ-OTDR) with
a spatial resolution of 100 m, [43]. The output power of the laser was 50 mW and its
linewidth was less than 3 kHz. In order to reach a long distance with the sensing of the
Φ-OTDR system, an EDFA and a distributed Raman ampliﬁcation were used. By the use
49
==PAGE:60==
3. Review of the State of the Art
of co-propagating Raman ampliﬁcation, an even signal power distribution is achieved in
order to ensure a signal-to-noise ratio for intrusion detection.
By the use of a Φ-OTDR with linearly chirped pulses and direct detection, such a system
has proven to measure the temperature and strain change with a resolution of 1 mK and
4nϵ, [44]. In the experiment described in [44], a ﬁbre of 1 km of length was investigated
with a spatial resolution of 10 m.
The Φ-OTDR dual pulse technique, as described in subsection 2.12.2, was experimen-
tally demonstrated in [45]. A high performance laser module, with a spectral bandwidth
of 2 kHz and a 10 dBm power level was used. The experimental results shows that
the sensing technique was capable of measuring a 230 Hz periodic perturbation along a
2 km long sensing ﬁbre with a spatial resolution of 5 m and a strain resolution of 10 nϵ, [45].
A modiﬁed version of the Φ-OTDR interferometry technique, as described in subsection
2.12.2, is presented in [46]. The proposed system was capable of quantifying multiple
dynamics along a 5 km sensing ﬁbre with a frequency resolution of 5 Hz, a spatial resolution
of 50 cm and a strain resolution of 40 nϵ, [46].
The Φ-OTDR electrical-domain technique, as described in subsection 2.12.2, is validated
in [47]. By the use of coherent Φ-OTDR it was shown that this sensing technique was
capable of spatially resolving 1 kHz dynamic strains along a 31 km long sensing ﬁbre with
a spatial resolution of 10 m and a strain resolution of 50 nϵ.
The sensing range of the Φ-OTDR electrical-domain technique has been proven to
be increased to 175 km in [48] with a 25 m spatial resolution, by the use of a
combination of co-pumping 2nd-order Raman ampliﬁcation, counter-pumping 1st-order
Raman ampliﬁcation and counter-pumping Brillouin ampliﬁcation. The co-pumping 2nd-
order Raman ampliﬁcation, which was based on random ﬁbre lasing, provides gain mainly
in the ﬁrst half of the whole span, then the counter-propagating Brillouin pump starts to
dominate the ampliﬁcation in the next 50 km segment and at last the 1st-order Raman
ampliﬁcation takes over the signal boosting in the last 37 km segment.
3.6
Magnetic Field Sensing
By the use of an all-ﬁbre optical magnetic ﬁeld sensor it was shown that the sensor can
have a sensitivity of 0.49 rad/T and it can measure magnetic ﬁelds from 0.02 to 3.2 T,
tesla, [49]. The mentioned sensor was based on Faraday rotation, a technique described
in section 2.14. The experimental conﬁguration used to test the magnetic ﬁeld sensor, as
described in [49], is seen in ﬁgure 3.1.
The ﬁbre Faraday rotator is a 2 cm long terbium-doped ﬁbre (Tb in ﬁgure 3.1), which is
spliced between the polarization maintaining ﬁbre (PM in ﬁgure 3.1) and a 1 m section of
polarizing ﬁbre (PZ in ﬁgure 3.1), passed through a magnet tube. The Verdet constant of
the terbium-doped ﬁbre is -24.5 ± 1.0 rad/(T m) at a wavelength of 1053 nm. Linearly
polarized light is launched into the polarizing maintaining ﬁbre. The polarization directions
50
==PAGE:61==
3. Review of the State of the Art
of the polarization maintaining ﬁbre and the polarizing ﬁbre are aligned with a rotational
diﬀerence set between 20° and 70° in order to obtain a nearly linear response curve of
the magnetic ﬁeld strength as a function of measured power. The magnet used is a
neodymium (N48 NdFeB) magnet of 4 cm with an inner and outer diameter of 5 mm and
6 cm, respectively. The magnetic ﬁeld imposed on the terbium-doped ﬁbre is changed by
translating the magnet along the length of the ﬁbre.
Figure 3.1: Experimental conﬁguration of an all-ﬁbre magnet sensor. Here PM
is polarization maintaining ﬁbre, PZ is polarizing ﬁbre, Tb is terbium-doped ﬁbre.
Figure from [49]
The axial component of the magnetic ﬁeld distribution along the central axis of the magnet
tube is calculated and presented along with the measured component in ﬁgure 3.2. In
ﬁgure 3.2 the physical ends of the magnet are also shown for reference. The averaged
magnetic density ﬂux Bav experienced by the 2 cm Tb ﬁbre is also presented in ﬁgure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Theoretical (solid line) and measured (circle) axial component of the
magnetic ﬁeld distribution along the central axis z of the magnet tube. The dashed
lines represent ends of the magnet and the dotted line represents theoretical Bav,
the magnetic density ﬂux averaged over a 2 cm length along the axis z. Figure
from [49]
51

4
PROBLEM STATEMENT
4.1
The Problem
Optical ﬁbres have many properties, some of them are their ability to measure changes in
loss of the signal send through them, change in temperature surrounding them, change in
vibration throughout the ﬁbre and change in the magnetic ﬁeld, if they are exposed to one.
Based on these phenomena this project will investigate the following problem;
To what degree can optical ﬁbres be used in connection with fault detection in submarine
power cables?
4.2
Project Description
The objective of this master project is to investigate to what degree optical ﬁbres can be
used in connection with fault detection in submarine power cables.
In the project the necessary background information will be given about the optical ﬁbres,
such as types, characteristics, the process of transmitting and receiving a signal and the
optical network components required for transmission over long distances.
Investigations of the response of optical ﬁbres to diﬀerent physical exposures under cables
faults is the main idea of the project.
State-of-the-art within diﬀerent methods, their properties, limitations and application
range will be given.
Main focus of the investigations is expected to be on Optical Time Domain Reﬂectometers
(OTDR) and the optical ﬁbre working as a temperature sensor.
The work will cover both literature studies and analytical approaches to promising solutions.
The ﬁndings can be validated by experimental investigations under laboratory conditions.
All the aspects and ﬁndings during the project are to be documented in a ﬁnal report. The
project includes:
• A literature review covering:
– Background information about the optical ﬁbre, including type, characteristic,
the process of transmitting and receiving a signal, and the components of an
optical network
– Optical ﬁbres sensitivity to changes in the physical environment
– State-of-the-art within diﬀerent methods of optical ﬁbres used for monitoring
and fault localisation, their properties, limitations and application range
• Solutions and recommendations
– Identiﬁcation of possible optical ﬁbres, signal type and network components
– Solutions utilizing dispersion, nonlinearities, losses and noise
57
==PAGE:68==
4. Problem Statement
• Reporting
The original project description can be found in appendix C.
4.3
Justiﬁcation
As described in chapter 3 many diﬀerent approaches have been suggested for optical ﬁbres
as a fault detection solution. None of the described methods in chapter 3 deal with optical
ﬁbres as a means for fault detection in submarine power cables of lengths of 760 km, as is
the case for VikingLink.
4.4
Relevance
Energinet plans a 1400MW, 525kV, HVDC connection between Great Britain and Denmark.
The project is being jointly developed and funded by National Grid and Energinet.dk.
The connection will consist of two submarine and underground cables and two converter
stations. The length of the connection is 760km (630km subsea) between Great Britain
and Denmark, [1].
Submarine cables of this length can be expected to experience several faults through their
lifetime. Monitoring of such cable systems can be done by means of optical ﬁbres and the
question is what the capabilities and limitations are of the ﬁbres and also what impact
cable faults might have on them.
58
==PAGE:69==
5
PROOF OF CONCEPT EXPERIMENTAL
INVESTIGATIONS UNDER LABORATORY
CONDITIONS
Proof of concept experiments have been executed in order to substantiate some of the
proposed solutions for fault detection presented in 2. Here OTDR and Brillouin temperature
sensing are investigated.
5.1
Test of Equipment
Before the experiments can be executed the equipment must be tested. The procedure for
this is presented in this section.
5.1.1
Objective
The objective is to test the output power and thereby ﬁnding the loss of the optical ﬁbres
and equipment at use for the following measurements and experiments.
5.1.2
Experimental Set-Up
The experimental set-up for the ﬁbre tests is schematically presented in ﬁgure 5.1. The
laser used is a tunable Yenista Optics laser, the power meter used is a Hewlett Packard
8153A and the ﬁbres under test are diﬀerent ﬁbres of diﬀerent lengths. The same laser
and power meter is used for all the tests of equipment.
Figure 5.1: Illustration of the experiment set-up for test of the optical ﬁbres at
use. The laser is connected to the optical ﬁbre under test, which is connected to
the power meter.
59
==PAGE:70==
5. Proof of Concept Experimental Investigations under Laboratory Conditions
Figure 5.2 presents photos of the set-up for the test of the optical ﬁbres.
Figure 5.2: Pictures of the experimental set-up for the test of the ﬁbre loss.
The laser is the box placed directly on the table and the laser is connected to the
ﬁbre coil containing the ﬁbre under test. The ﬁbre is connected to the power
meter through a photodiode, which is the black cylinder placed on the table. The
photodiode transmits an electrical signal of the output power from the ﬁbre to the
power meter, which is the box placed on top of the laser.
The set-up for the test of the isolator is presented in ﬁgure 5.3.
Figure 5.3: Illustration of the experimental set-up for the test of the isolator at
use. The laser is connected to the isolator by the use of a SMF patch cord which
again is connected to the power meter by a SMF patch cord.
The set-up for the test of the circulator is presented in ﬁgure 5.4.
Figure 5.4: Illustration of the experimental set-up for the test of the circulator
at use. The laser is connected to the circulator by the use of a SMF patch cord
which again is connected to the power meter by a SMF patch cord.
5.1.3
Execution
The execution of the test of the optical ﬁbres is as follows; the power meter is turned on
and so is the laser. The laser is set to a wavelength of 1550 nm, and a power of 10 dBm is
sent into the ﬁbre under test. The output power from the ﬁbre is then measured on the
power meter.
60
==PAGE:71==
5. Proof of Concept Experimental Investigations under Laboratory Conditions
The execution of the test of the isolator is similar to the test of the optical ﬁbres. The
power meter is turned on and so is the laser. The laser is set to a wavelength of 1550 nm,
and a power of 5 dBm is sent into the isolator. The output power from the isolator is then
measured on the power meter.
In a similar way the test of the circulator is conducted. Here the laser is ﬁrst connected to
the input A and the power meter is connected to the B port, as seen in ﬁgure 5.4. The
loss is measured and the laser is moved to the B port and the power meter is moved to the
C output.
5.1.4
Data Treatment and Results
For the tests of the optical ﬁbres the total loss through each of the optical ﬁbres is estimated
by the use of equation 2.13, as αL = Pin −Pout. With the ﬁbres being single-mode ﬁbres
(SMFs) the attenuation coeﬃcient is set to 0.2 dB/km as presented in subsection 2.4.1.
The estimated loss and the measured loss for the ﬁbres under test are all presented in table
5.1.

from the speciﬁcations, and presented in equations 5.7 and 5.8, is used. Here it is seen

such accuracy is not needed.

ﬁbre and where on the ﬁbre a possible break or fault occurs. Due to these considerations
the length of ﬁbre number four will be refereed to as
17.976 km ± 0.001 km
(5.9)
The attenuation is given as 0.198 ± 0.001 dB/km. This indicates that the ﬁbre is either a
SMF, whose attenuation is 0.2 dB/km or a NZDSF, whose attention is also 0.2 dB/km, as
presented in subsection 2.7.1 and A.6.4 respectively.
5.3
Cable Break Measurement using OTDR
In this experiment the OTDR is used to determine inﬂuence of a break in the transmitted
power in an optical ﬁbre, under the assumption that if the submarine power cable breaks,
so does the optical ﬁbre. In the experiment the ﬁbre is not broken, but bended in a smaller
and smaller radius.
5.3.1
Objective
As discussed in subsection 2.4.2 the bend of an optical ﬁbre may cause the light ray to hit
the core-cladding interface at an angle greater than the critical angle and thus the light
ray cannot experience total internal reﬂection. This bend and consequential loss will cause
a change in the reﬂected Rayleigh scattering and can be measured with the OTDR. The
objective is that the OTDR is able to measure the inﬂuence of the bend before all of the
light is cut oﬀat a small bending radius.
5.3.2
Experimental Set-Up
The experimental set-up for the cable break experiment is schematically presented in ﬁgure
5.8. The same OTDR as used for the black box experiment presented in section 5.2 is used.
The OTDR is connected to optical ﬁbre number one. Fibre number one is then connected
to a one meter patch cord SMF which can be coiled around cylinders of diﬀerent diameters.
The cylinders and their diameters in mm are pictured in ﬁgure 5.9. Here the patch cord is
coiled around the 35 mm diameter cylinder and taped down in order to stay where it is
positioned. The patch cord is connected to optical ﬁbre number four. The output power
at the end of ﬁbre number four is attenuated by a ﬁbre cap.
66
==PAGE:77==
5. Proof of Concept Experimental Investigations under Laboratory Conditions
Figure 5.8: Illustration of the experimental set-up for the cable break experiment.
The OTDR is connected to optical ﬁbre no. 1, which is connected to a one meter
patch cord of SMF. The patch cord can be coiled around cylinders of diﬀerent
diameters and is then connected to optical ﬁbre no. 4 a 17.976 km ± 0.001 km
ﬁbre. Attenuation at the end of ﬁbre no. 4 is achieved by the use of a ﬁbre cap.

it is positioned.

As seen in ﬁgure 5.14, 5.15 and 5.16, already at the bend diameter of 15 mm, the reﬂected
power after the bend is strongly aﬀected. By the use of this technique, it is possible to
detect where the bend or break of the ﬁbre is located, with the use of a reference OTDR
trace and by measuring the reﬂected power before and after the break. Even though the
dead zones are larger than the break area, it is still possible to measure an impact in the
subsequent reﬂected power trace and from this deduce where the break occurred.
5.4
Temperature Measurement
In this experiment the Brillouin frequency shift is measured as a function of temperature,
with inspiration from the methods presented in subsection 2.11.1 and in [24], but without
the counterpropagating cw laser pump.
5.4.1
Objective
The objectives for this experiment is that a power cable fault will emit heat and thus
a change in the temperature surrounding the optical ﬁbre placed in close proximity to
the power cable. The optical ﬁbre is able to measure a change in the temperature and
therefore able to detect a fault.
The Brillouin shift in MHz as a function of temperature should according to [24] have a
linear relationship and this is in this experiment investigated.
The temperature emitted during a power cable fault of an underwater electrical discharge
can be as high as in the range of 30,000 K, [71].
The tolerable operating temperature of a power cable refers to the conductor and it depends
on the type of dielectric in the cable, [71]. For dielectrics of MI and XLPE, as used for the
VikingLink, the tolerable operating temperature is 90 ℃.
73
==PAGE:84==
5. Proof of Concept Experimental Investigations under Laboratory Conditions
5.4.2
Experimental Set-Up
The experimental set-up for the temperature experiment is schematically presented in
ﬁgure 5.17.
Figure 5.17:
Illustration of the experimental set-up for the temperature
experiment. The laser is connected to an isolator which is connected to a circulator
by input A. At port B the circulator is connected to optical ﬁbre number 2, a 34
km SMF. The optical ﬁbre is placed in an oven and a thermometer is used to
measure the temperature of the ﬁbre. At output C an optical spectrum analyser
(OSA) is connected to detect the backscattered spectrum from the optical ﬁbre.
The laser is the same as used in the previous experiments. The laser is connected to the
isolator, which makes sure no backscattering reaches the laser. The isolator is connected to
the circulator by input A. By port B of the circulator optical ﬁbre number 2 is connected.
The optical ﬁbre is placed in an oven. The oven is a Binder FED 400 and it has a hole
on the side, where the optical ﬁbre can be transported in and out of the oven, without
opening the oven door, as seen in ﬁgure 5.18.

By the end of ﬁbre number two attenuation is achieved by a ﬁbre cap. The optical
spectrum analyzer (OSA) used is an Anritsu MS9740A and it is connected to output C of
the circulator in order to measure the backscattered spectrum from ﬁbre number two.
5.4.3
Execution
At a room temperature of 21 ℃, the oven is not yet turned on and the Brillouin scattering
from optical ﬁbre number 2 is measured by the OSA. First the OSA is turned on and then
the laser is turned on to a wavelength of 1550 nm and an output power of 6 dBm. Spectra
of the Brillouin backscatter is collected, one every minute for ten minutes, by the OTDR.
The Anti-Stokes peak wavelength positions are measured and plotted as a function of time,
in order to secure that the temperature of the ﬁbre is stable. This is presented in ﬁgure
5.19.
Figure 5.19: Anti-Stokes peak position in wavelength, of the backscattered signal,
measured every minute for ten minutes, at room temperature of 21 ℃.
From ﬁgure 5.19, the temperature is assessed to be stable, as the range of the Anti-Stokes
peak position in wavelength is within a range of 0.001 nm, and the spectra can be used as
the reference temperature measurements. The spectra can be used because the change in
temperature of the position of the Anti-Stokes peak position is seen in the ﬁrst and second
decimal number on the nm wavelength.
The oven is turned on to 30 ℃. When both the oven thermometer and the thermometer
inside the oven reache 30 ℃, measurements, of the backscattered spectrum, is taken
every third minute for 30 minutes. From the backscattered spectra, the Anti-Stokes peak
wavelength positions are plotted as a function of time. This is again done to check if the
desired temperature of the ﬁbre is stable. The Anti-Stokes peak wavelength position is
assessed to be stable, as the range of the Anti-Stokes peak position in wavelength is within
a range of 0.001 nm, and ten measurement of the backscattered signal is made with the
OSA.
The same procedure, with temperature tests before measuring the spectra used to measure
the Brillouin frequency shift as a function of temperature, is executed for temperatures of
40 ℃, 50 ℃and 55 ℃.
The Anti-Stokes peak positions in wavelengths as a function of time, for temperatures 21

From equation 5.39, the slope of the linear ﬁt is 21.28 MHz/℃. This is roughly a magnitude
larger than the expected values of 1 MHz/℃. A calculation, measuring or systematic error
might have occurred, but was not found or detected.
As stated earlier, the counterpropagating cw laser pump is not included in the experimental
set-up. This choice was made in order to simplify the set-up. Therefore the measured
Brillouin frequency shift temperature dependence cannot be compared with those presented
in the literature. Instead a tendency was detected. A tendency, which indicates, that the
Brillouin backscattered signal can indeed be used for measuring a change in temperature.
In accordance with [24] a linear relationship between the Brillouin shift in MHz and
temperature is found.
81
==PAGE:92==
6
SOLUTIONS
In this chapter solutions to the problem stated in chapter 4, of to what degree optical ﬁbres
can be used in connection with fault detection in submarine power cables, are presented.
6.1
Fibre Type
The ﬁbre types most useful for the problem of detecting and monitoring submarine power
cables with optical ﬁbres are presented. The ﬁbre types are presented for the diﬀerent
detection and monitoring methods.
6.1.1
Scattering
As presented in chapter 3, the optical ﬁbres used for OTDRs, temperature sensing and
vibration sensing are single-mode optical ﬁbres. The most commonly deployed single mode
ﬁbre is the ITU-T G.652, as presented in subsection 2.7.1. An example of the ITU-T G.652
ﬁbre is Corning’s SMF-28e, [9], which has an eﬀective group index of ngeff = 1.4682 for a
wavelength of 1550 nm.
6.1.2
Magnetic Field Sensing
For magnetic ﬁeld detection a ﬁbre with a high Verdet constant is needed, as presented in
subsection 2.6.1. The Verdet constant in Terbium-doped glass is reported to be high and
useful for measuring the Faraday eﬀect and thereby the strenght of the magnetic ﬁeld, [74].
For a terbium-doped ﬁbre a Verdet constant of -24.5 ± 1.0 rad/(Tm) at 1053 nm can be
achieved, [49].
As an alternative to the Terbium doped ﬁbre is Thorlab’s SHB1500 Spun High Birefringence
(HiBi), [75].
Besides the ﬁbre with a high Verdet constant, a polarization maintaining ﬁbre and a
polarizing ﬁbre is needed, in order to maintain and detect the polarization, as presented in
section 3.6.
6.2
Signal Type
The most promising signal types, for the diﬀerent detection and monitoring methods,
discussed throughout this report, are presented below.

Phase-OTDR
As described in subsection 2.12.2, the laser source needed for the phase-OTDR conﬁguration

the ﬁbre length, the linewidth, or optical bandwidth, have to be 0.00015 MHz or 0.15 kHz,
to have a coherence length of 636 km, [78].

6.3
Optical Network Components
In this section the most promising optical network components are presented.
6.3.1
Transmitters
In this subsection the most promising optical transmitters used to the detection and sensing
techniques for fault detection, localization and monitoring are presented.
For both the pulsed laser and the plane linear polarisation signal, some of the requirements
needed for the signal to be used to detect faults and monitor power cables are presented in
subsection 2.8.1. All of the requirements, including the ones presented in subsection 2.8.1,
are as follows;
• High Eﬃciency
A high eﬃciency is always desirable as more output is produced, with less input.
The wall-plug eﬃciency of a laser, is the total electric-to-optical power eﬃciency,
which is the ratio of optical output power to consumed electrical input power, [79].
Semiconductor p −n junction laser diodes, as presented in subsection 2.8.1 The
p −n Junction, can reach electrical-to-optical eﬃciencies in the order of 50 %, [80].
• Good Reliability
A high reliability and a long life time of a laser, is of importance, as the laser system
87
==PAGE:98==
6. Solutions
will last longer. A longer lasting laser system, with a high reliability, means less
repairs and updates, and thus a cheaper laser system, all in all.
• The Right Wavelength Range
It is desirable to have a laser that emits the desired wavelength of 1550 nm.
• Possibility of Direct Modulation
The possibility to implement direct modulation, in the form of pulsed lasers, as
presented in subsection 2.8.1 Pulsed Laser, is crucial for the of the scattering
detections, with timing, systems, as presented in section 2.10 to 2.12.
• Compact in Size
The semiconductor lasers are small in size, [6]. A compact size is desirable for a
transmitter, as it therefore takes up less space in the set-up. Furthermore the size of
the transmitted laser signal should be compatible with the ﬁbre core size, [3].
• High Emitted Power
The emitted power is dependent on the internal quantum eﬃciency, as presented in
equation A.115, and thereby dependent on the diﬀerential quantum eﬃciency, as seen
in equation A.117. Through the diﬀerential quantum eﬃciency the emitted power is
also dependent on the external quantum eﬃciency, presented in equation A.118. And
by the external quantum eﬃciency the emitted power is dependent on the bandgap
energy and the applied voltage to the laser, as seen in equation A.121. Thus a large
diﬀerential quantum eﬃciency and a large total quantum eﬃciency secure a high
emitted power.
A high emitted power is needed, as this ensures a long transmission distance, as the
emitted power of the laser is the input power to the ﬁbre. The output power of the
ﬁbre depends on the input power as presented in equation 2.13.
• Large SNR
From subsection 2.8.1, Signal-to-Noise Ratio it is seen that the signal-to-noise ra-
tio (SNR) becomes higher with lower noise. A large SNR is desirable as this indicates
a low noise and thus a large signal. The noise in a transmitter will, over long dis-
tances, inﬂuence the signal, so that at the receiver end, the signal may not be readable.
• Small Linewidth
From subsection 2.8.1, Spectral Linewidth the deﬁnition of the spectral linewidth
is presented. A small spectral linewidth is favourable as broadening of the signal, in
the form of dispersion, will take a longer travel distance to aﬀect the signal. The
dispersion is presented and discussed in section 2.3.
As stated in subsection 2.8.1, these requirements are best met by semiconductors such as
light-emitting diodes, (LEDs) and semiconductor lasers.
88
==PAGE:99==
6. Solutions
Pulsed Lasers
As presented in section 6.2, the Rayleigh, Brillouin and Raman sensing techniques all
utilize pulsed lasers for signal generation. The physics and mechanisms behind the pulsed
lasers are presented in subsection 2.8.1 Pulsed Lasers.
The requirements for the pulsed laser are, besides the requirements mentioned in subsection
6.3.1, a fast rise and fall time in the pulses, but also a substantial temporal width, as the
power of the Rayleigh backscattered signal is proportional to the temporal width, as seen
in equation 2.16.
The length of the pulse width determines how much energy is injected into the optical
ﬁbre. Larger pulse widths will correspond to greater dynamic range, [81] and thus the
possibility of detecting faults and monitor ﬁbre over longer distances. But choosing a pulse
width too long will result in longer dead zones for an OTDR, as described in subsection
2.10.1. A standard pulse width is often from nanoseconds to microseconds, [18].
A comparison of some of the pros and cons of the pulsed laser types presented in subsection
2.8.1 Pulsed Lasers is seen in table 6.1.
Table 6.1: Pros and cons of the pulsed laser types presented in in subsection
2.8.1 Pulsed Lasers.
Pulsed Laser Type
Pros
Cons

[82]

energy, the shorter
the pulse build-up time,
and consequently the required

combination with either

From table 6.1 the pros for the Q-switching pulsed laser types is seen to be advantageous,
as the pulse duration is in the nanosecond range and it has a fast build-up time. As a
consequence, Q-switching is seen to be the most promising solution for pulsed lasers used
for fault detection, localization and monitoring of power cables.
Plane Linear Polarization
As presented in section 6.2 the preferable signal type for sensing the magnetic ﬁeld of the
power cable by use of an optical ﬁbre, is linear polarized laser light of 1550 nm.
In general all laser light is linear polarized, [6], and thus ﬁnding the most promising laser
is a matter of ﬁnding a laser which fulﬁls the requirements presented above. Such a laser
could be the semiconductor laser, where some of the common materials are, [85]
89
==PAGE:100==
6. Solutions
• GaAs (gallium arsenide)
• AlGaAs (aluminum gallium arsenide)
• GaP (gallium phosphide)
• InGaP (indium gallium phosphide)
• GaN (gallium nitride)
• InGaAs (indium gallium arsenide)
• GaInNAs (indium gallium arsenide nitride)
• InP (indium phosphide)
• GaInP (gallium indium phosphide)
These materials all have a direct bandgap, which is discussed in subsection A.7.6.
6.3.2
Receivers
In this subsection the requirements, and a suggestion, for the optical receivers used for fault
localisation and monitoring of submarine power cables are presented. The requirements
are, [3];
• High Sensitivity
As presented in subsection 2.8.2 p −i −n Receivers the sensitivity of a receiver can
be described by the noise equivalent power (NEP), which is the input signal power
that results in a SNR of 1 in a 1 Hz output bandwidth, [14].
Furthermore the receiver sensitivity can be described, as in subsection A.8.5, as the
minimum average power needed to keep the BER below a certain value, often a
1 × 10−9, [3].
No matter the deﬁnition of the receiver sensitivity, a high sensitivity is needed, as
more laser light can be detected and thus a more accurate measurement can be made.
• Fast Response
The response of the receiver is the speed at which the receiver varies with the varia-
tions of the incident optical power, [3], as described in subsection 2.8.2 Tise Time
and Bandwidth.
The responsitivity of the receiver is described in subsection 2.8.2 Responsivity and
Quantum Eﬃciency and can be described in term of the quantum eﬃciency.
A fast response of a receiver is desirable, as light can be converted to electrical form
faster and thus a more accurate measurement can be made.
• Low Noise
The noises in optical receivers are described in subsection 2.8.2 Receiver Noise.
The performance and noise in the receiver are often measured in SNR and NEP, both
presented and described in subsection 2.8.2 p −i −n Receivers.
The noise in the receiver inﬂuences the conversion of the optical signal to electric
form. With a low noise a more accurate measurement can be made.
• Small Temperature Sensitivity
Many of the characteristics of a photodetector receiver are aﬀected by changes in
temperature, [86].
90
==PAGE:101==
6. Solutions
The responsivity varies due to decrease or increase of the bandgap in the semicon-
ductor, which varies due to decrease or increase in the temperature, [86].
It is therefore crucial for the best possible operation of the receiver, to have as small
a temperature sensitivity as possible, as it otherwise will aﬀect the characteristics of
the receiver, and thereby aﬀect the accuracy of the measurements.
• High Reliability
As for the transmitter, a high reliability and a long life time of a photodetector is
important, as the laser system will last longer.
• Small in Size
As for the transmitter, a small size is desirable as it takes up less space in the set-up.
The receiver size should also ensure easy coupling to optical ﬁbres, [13].
The requirements stated above for optical receivers are best met by semiconductor pho-
todetectors, [3].
From ﬁgure 2.23 it is seen that Si is an excellent choice for detector material for detection
in the 0.8 - 0.85 µ m wavelength window, but it cannot be used for the 1.3 - 1.5 µ m wave-
length window, where the optical ﬁbres have a lower attenuation. As stated in subsection
6.2.1 through 6.2.4 a signal type of 1550 nm is desirable for the fault detection methods
suggested. For this longer wavelength, semiconductors with lower energy bandgap must
be used, and from ﬁgure 2.23 it is seen that InGaAs or InGaAsP are the preferred materials.
As discussed in 2.8.2 APD Receivers, the APD generally provide a higher SNR, due to
the internal gain in the receiver.
A receiver for the fault detection and localization methods presented, could be the one
presented in subsection 6.2.4, which is the InGaAs Avalanche Photodetector from Thorlabs,
[76]. It meets the requirements of the preferred material, the high SNR and the requirements
stated above.
6.4
Management
In this section compensation management for dispersion, nonlinearities and loss are
presented.
6.4.1
Dispersion
Dispersion is presented in section 2.3. Dispersion is an issue for optical communication
systems when several pulses are transmitted in a bit stream and the dispersion spreads
out the signals over several bits. This is not an issue in the scattering detection methods,
previously described, as they only use a single pulse at a time. An issue would occur if the
intensity of the pulse is to become lower than what is detectable for the receiver, due to
dispersion.
91
==PAGE:102==
6. Solutions
For magnetic ﬁeld testing the signal is not a pulse, but a linear polarised continuous laser
light.
The choice of optical ﬁbre, for the fault detection methods using scattering, is singlemode
ﬁbre. From equation A.25 the broadening of the pulse by dispersion, in an optical ﬁbre can
be calculated. For a 630 km Corning SMF-28e ﬁbre with a pulsed laser with a bandwidth
of 1 nm, [87], the pulse broadening becomes
∆T = LD∆λ = 630 × 18.0 × 1 = 11340 ps
(6.8)
Which mean that the pulse will spread from a bandwidth of 1 nm to a bandwidth of 3.402
m.
When a singlemode ﬁbre is installed in a ﬁbre link, the dispersion can be compensated by
the use of a dispersion compensation ﬁlter. This ﬁlter is often a dispersion compensating
ﬁbre (DCF), a dispersion shifted ﬁbre (DSF) or a dispersion ﬂattered ﬁbre (DFF). Some
of the ﬁbre types are presented in section 2.7.
By doping the core and the cladding regions, [3], or even designing a triple cladding proﬁle,
[88], large negative values of dispersion can be reached in a DCF. The large negative values
of dispersion are accomplished by a price of higher loss, nonlinearities are easier reached
and the ﬁbre is sensitive to bending loss, [88].
In ﬁgure 6.5, the local dispersion and the accumulated dispersion in a ﬁbre link are
presented. The ﬁbre link contains SMFs (red in ﬁgure 6.5), DCFs (blue in ﬁgure 6.5) and
ampliﬁers. Dispersion compensating ﬁbres are typically placed within two ampliﬁers, [88].
Figure 6.5:
Local dispersion and accumulated dispersion for a ﬁbre link
containing SMFs, DCFs and ampliﬁers. Figure from [88].
The accumulated dispersion in the SMF of 630 km is
92
==PAGE:103==
6. Solutions
DSMF × LSMF = 18.0 × 630 = 11340 ps
nm
(6.9)
In order to calculate the length of the DCF needed to compensate for this dispersion, the
following equation needs to be satisﬁed, [4]
DSMF × LSMF + DDCF × LDCF = 0 ps
nm
(6.10)
With DDCF as the dispersion value of the DCF, in units of ps/(nm × km) and LDCF as
the length of the dispersion compensating ﬁbre, measured in km.
It is assumed that dispersion compensating ﬁbres work bidirectionally. They can on this
basis be used for all the sensing and fault localization techniques using scattering, as the
signal and scattering propagates bidirectional in the ﬁbre.
Thorlabs have created a ﬁbre, the DCF38, which is speciﬁcally designed to compensate
Corning’s SMF-28e ﬁbre, [89], with a dispersion parameter at 1550 nm of -38 ps/(nm ×
km).
The length needed of Thorlab’s DCF38 can be calculated using equation 6.10, and will
result in
(18.0 × 427.5) + (−38 × 202.5) = 0
(6.11)
But as the scattering products on the market are able to detect at long distances, as seen
in table 3.4, it is assumed that they are compensating for dispersion by the use of computer
programs within the products.
As presented in subsection 6.1.2, the ﬁbre of choice for the magnetic ﬁeld sensing techniques
is a ﬁbre with a high Verdet constant, such as Thorlab’s SHB1500 Spun High Birefringence
(HiBi) ﬁbre, [75]. On the speciﬁcation sheet for the ﬁbre the dispersion parameter is not
presented.
6.4.2
Nonlinearities
If the electromagnetic ﬁeld is high and intense enough, any dielectric will response with
the light becoming nonlinear and optical ﬁbres are no exception, [3].
Both Brillouin and Raman scattering are nonlinear eﬀects and are both presented in section
2.5. In general terms, nonlinear optical eﬀects can limit the performance of an optical
system, [4].
This section will focus on managing the nonlinear eﬀects.
Often the nonlinear eﬀects can be managed by implementing a power limitation for the
transmission system. By securing a power level of the optical signal, thought out length of
the transmission, that is both above the noise limitations and below the nonlinear eﬀects
limitations, a detectable signal can be incident on the receiver. This is schematically
presented in ﬁgure 6.6.
93
==PAGE:104==
6. Solutions
Figure 6.6: Power limitation in a transmission system. Figure from [4].
From equation 6.4 the threshold power for Brillouin scattering, for a ﬁbre of 630 km, is ≈
2.04 dBm. Brillouin scattering is needed for some of the temperature detections methods
presented.
From equation 6.7 the threshold power for Raman scattering is calculated to 20.18 dBm,
for a ﬁbre of 630 km. Raman scattering is not desired in any of the detection methods
presented, is is only desired in Raman ampliﬁers.
6.4.3
Ampliﬁers
The need for ampliﬁers is discussed in section 2.9.
In this section the requirement for ampliﬁers, used for fault detection and localization,
over long distances are discussed. The requirements are as follows;
• High Gain
The gain in an optical ampliﬁer is the ratio of output power to input power, measured
in dB, [90]. A high gain is essential for a good ampliﬁer, so the signal, incident on
the ampliﬁer, is ampliﬁed, to compensate for the loss.
• High Output Power
A high output power for an ampliﬁer is needed, as the incident power on the ampliﬁer
is low and it is the job of the ampliﬁer to increase the power, to a high output power.
Furthermore a high emitted power is needed, as this ensures a long transmission
distance.
• The Right Wavelength Range
For the case of fault localization and detection, only a single wavelength is used,
which is 1550 nm. Therefore it is of great importance that the ampliﬁer can amplify
this wavelength.
• Polarization Insensitivity
In the detection technique of detecting the magnetic ﬁeld of the power cable with an
optical ﬁbre, the change in polarisation of the incident light is measured, as presented
in section 2.14. For this detection scheme it is of utter most importance that any
ampliﬁer does not alter the polarization of the incident signal.
EFDAs are polarization insensitive, but Raman ampliﬁers are not, [6].
94
==PAGE:105==
6. Solutions
• High Eﬃciency
A high eﬃciency is always desirable as more output is produced, with less input.
The gain eﬃciency is the gain as a function of input power in dB/mW, [90].
EDFAs usually have a high power transfer eﬃciency from pump to signal often above
50 %, [90] and the best gain eﬃciencies are ≈10 dB/mW, [6].
For the EDFA the most eﬃcient pumping wavelengths, for ampliﬁcation to 1550 nm,
are 1480 nm and 980 nm, as presented in ﬁgure A.50. The two level pumping scheme,
achieved with a 1480 nm pump, is less eﬃcient than the three level pumping scheme,
implemented at 980 nm, because the gain per unit pump power is lover and the noise
is higher, [6]. On the other hand, the pump transition linewidth and saturation signal
power are greater at 1480 nm, therefore pumping at this wavelength can be used for
higher power ampliﬁers. In some cases, the EDFAs are simultaneously pumped at
both wavelengths, [6].
The Raman ampliﬁer gain eﬃciency in germanium-doped silica ﬁbre is ≈0.02 dB/mW,
[6]. The eﬃciency of the Raman ampliﬁer is lower, than that oﬀered by the EDFA,
but it can be enhanced by the use of dispersion compensating ﬁbre (DCF), along
with a high power laser and thus making it comparable with the EDFAs, [6].
• Low Noise
Noise in both EDFAs and Raman ampliﬁers are presented in subsection 2.9.1
Ampliﬁer Noise and subsection 2.9.2 Noise in Raman Ampliﬁers, respectively.
A low noise in an ampliﬁer is preferable, as the receiver in the end of the optical
system, has to be able to detect the signal, and the less noise added to the ampliﬁed
signal, the easier the signal is to detect.
As presented in subsection 2.9.1 Ampliﬁer Noise, the ampliﬁer noise ﬁgure is a
quantity of how degraded the SNR is though the ampliﬁer. The noise ﬁgure for large
ampliﬁers, as presented in subsection 2.9.1 Ampliﬁer Noise, is Fn ≈2nsp. The
spontaneous emission factor nsp, is presented in subsection 2.9.1 Ampliﬁer Noise
as nsp = N2/(N2 −N1). The best case for large gain ampliﬁers, [4] is
Fn = SNRin
SNRout
≈2
(6.12)
Fn,dB =10 × log(Fn) = 3 dB
(6.13)
which corresponds to complete population inversion, with N1 = 0 and nsp = 1.
The eﬀective noise ﬁgure of a Raman ampliﬁer is presented in subsection 2.9.2 Noise
in Raman Ampliﬁers, as Feff = Fnexp(−αsL). The eﬀective noise ﬁgure is often
below 1, [4], and this makes the Raman ampliﬁer attractive for transmission over
long distances, [3].
Advantages of EDFAs
• High gain
• High power transfer eﬃciency
• High output power
• Low noise ﬁgure
95
==PAGE:106==
6. Solutions
• Suitable for long transmission applications
• Polarization insensitive
Disadvantages of EDFAs
• EDFAs are not small devices and they cannot be integrated with other semiconductor
devices, [90].
• EDFAS exhibit ASE. Even if there is no input signal to the EDFA, ASE will still be
transmitted as output signal from the EDFA.
• Gain saturation
Advantages of Raman Ampliﬁers
The advantages of the Raman ampliﬁer relative to the EDFA are;
• Arbitrary ﬁbre host
• Wide bandwidth
Disadvantages of Raman Ampliﬁers
The disadvantages of the Raman ampliﬁer relative to the EDFA are;
• Small gain
• Greater pump power and lower eﬃciency
• Longer ﬁbre lengths
• Polarization sensitive
All the ampliﬁer solutions presented above, need a power supply. In order to secure a
power supply for the optical ampliﬁer in constellation with a submarine power cable, a
separate power supply for the ampliﬁers is recommended.
96
==PAGE:107==

The Anritsu is mainly chosen because it is an C-OTDR. The C-OTDR is presented in
subsection 2.10.3. The advantages of the C-OTDR is the possibility to amplify both the
launched test-signal and the backscattered signal by the use of a ﬁbre pair and optical
feed back paths within the repeaters. But as seen in table 6.2 the event dead zone of
the Anritsu is 500 meters. The event dead zone is presented and discussed in subsection
2.10.1 Event Dead Zone. The disadvantage of this long event dead zone is that for every
repeater in the C-OTDR there will be 500 meters after the repeater where an accurate
length measurement cannot be performed. This means in practice, that a fault in the event
dead zone may not be easily located by the advantages of the OTDR system. Furthermore
the Anritsu has a 15 minutes measuring time, which again is a disadvantage in practice.
The longer the measuring time, the longer the man-hours using the device.
97
==PAGE:108==
6. Solutions
A better choice of OTDR could be the ABSYS or the YOKOGAWA, as they both have a
much smaller event dead zone, of 1 meter and 0.6 meters, respectively, as seen in table 6.2.
They both have a distance range of 512 km. This is not long enough to cover the entire
VikingLink of 630 km, but can still be used if two OTDRs are used, one sending a test-signal
from each end of the link.
The disadvantage of the ABSYS is the long attenuation dead zone of 10 meters. The
attenuation dead zone is presented in subsection 2.10.1 Attenuation Dead Zone. This
means in practice that there will be a distance of 10 meters after, for example a connection,
at which an accurate loss measurement cannot be performed and therefore a fault cannot
be easily located by the OTDR system. It is advantageous to have as short dead zones as
possible.
This is an advantage that the YOKOGAWA OTDR has, with both a short event dead
zone and a short attenuation dead zone, of 0.6 meters and 4 meters, respectively. The
disadvantage of the YOKAGAWA is the long sampling resolution of 32 meters. The
sampling resolution is presented in 2.10.2. Having a long sampling resolution result in a
longer distance between sampling points and therefore a longer distance over which a fault
can happen, without the OTDR is able to detect it.
The EXFO has a short sampling resolution, of 10 meters. The distance range of the
EXFO is only 400 km, but this can be compensated for with testing from each end of the
VikingLink. Another advantage of the EXFO is that is has short event zones of 1 meter
for the event dead zone and 5 meters for the attenuation dead zone. The disadvantage of
the EXFO is the measuring time, which is from 5 seconds all the way to 60 minutes.
The uncertainties for the four chosen OTDRs are calculated for half of the VikingLink
distance, that being 315 km of the submarine power cable, and presented in table 6.3.
Here it is seen that the Anritsu has the smallest uncertainty, at ±10 meters ±0.16 meters
and the the EXFO comes in second at ±13.90 meters.
Taking the the long event dead zone of 500 meters, which the Anritsu has, into account,
the disadvantages for the Anritsu are found to be to large for the purpose of detection
faults in a submarine power cable.
Therefore the most promising OTDR, available on the market, for fault localization in
submarine power cables, is the EXFO FTB-7600-E UHD OTDR, [51].
With the only disadvantage of a measurement time that can reach one hour.
In table 3.1 the state of the art for fault locations by the use of OTDR, Brillouin backscatter
and Raman OFDR are presented. The only solution that can be compared with the available
OTDR products on the market is the OTDR and C-OTDR solution installed between
the Norwegian mainland and Svalbard, [34]. In the article the length of the optical ﬁbre
connection is presented, but not the sampling resolution, the uncertainty, dead zones or
the measuring time. It is therefore not possible to compare this solution to any of the
available OTDR or C-OTDR products on the market.

6.6
Optical Fibre as Temperature Sensor
In table 3.5 and 3.6 the state of the art solutions for fault location using optical ﬁbres, by
means of temperature and strain and vibration, available on the market, are presented.
From these tables, four products for temperature measurement have been chosen and they
are presented in table 6.4.
The ﬁrst criteria used for choosing the temperature measurement products from table 3.5
and table 3.6, is that it should have a long distance range.
The OZ Optics DSTS BOTDA (Distributed Strain and Temperature Sensor) (Brillouin
Optical Domain Analysis) has a measurement range of 100 km, [58]. B-OTDA is presented
in subsection 2.11.1.
Omnisens DITEST-L Interrogator DTS DSS (Distributed Temperature Sensor) (Distributed
Strain Sensor) has a measuring range of 60 km and a maximum of 120 km ﬁbre distance,
as the sensor conﬁguration is a loop conﬁguration, [64].
The AP Sensing and Sensa DTS both have a measurement range of 50 km.
Neither of the products have a distance range that can cover the entire VikingLink of 630
km, nor can any of them be used, if two products are set in use, one in each end.
Not taking the VikingLink into consideration, the best temperature sensor product, of the
four presented in table 6.4, is the OZ Optics DSTS BOTDA. It has the longest distance
range of 100 km, the smallest spatial resolution of 0.1 meters, the largest temperature range
of -270 ℃to +1000 ℃, the smallest temperature resolution of 0.005 ℃and a measuring
time as short as 1 second.

6. Solutions
6.9
Magnetic Field Sensing
In table 3.2 the state of the art solution for fault localization by magnetic ﬁeld sensing
with optical ﬁbres is presented. Many issues arise from this sensing type and some of them
are listed below;
• The length presented as state of the art only has 2 cm of the optical ﬁbre inside the
magnetic ﬁeld.
• The optical ﬁbre has to be placed in the direction of the magnetic ﬁeld as presented in
ﬁgure 2.45. As a consequence, the optical ﬁbre has to be spun around the submarine
power cable, as seen in ﬁgure 2.47, ﬁgure 2.48 and ﬁgure 2.49, leading to the need
of a much longer optical ﬁbre, as compared to the power cable. It also leads to
challenges when producing the power cable with the optical ﬁbre spun around it.
• It is not possible to localise the position of the change in the magnetic ﬁeld.
Non of the proposed magnetic ﬁeld detection schemes, as presented in subsection
2.14, have localisation detection. All of the detection techniques measure the average
magnetic ﬁeld from an optical ﬁbre coiled around a conductor. Not knowing the
position of the change in magnetic ﬁeld undermines the entire purpose of using
magnetic ﬁeld as a fault localisation technique.
• With the optical ﬁbre spun around the power cable, the advantage of utilising more
than one sensing technique vanishes, as all the other sensing techniques have the
optical ﬁbre placed alongside the length of the power cable. This is under the
assumption that more than one optical ﬁbre is used and that they all are placed
together in the same jacket.
• With the optical ﬁbre spun around the power cable, the option of having an optical
communication links alongside the optical ﬁbre, in the same jacket, is limited. This
possibility will be limited as the extended length of the optical ﬁbre will cause further
loss and attenuation to the optical communication link and therefore a larger number
of ampliﬁers would be needed.
6.10
Fibre Placement in the Submarine Power Cables
In order to use the advantage of the OTDR and with the assumption that if the power
cables breaks, so does the optical ﬁbre, the optical ﬁbre must be placed as close to the
power cables as possible. For temperature, strain and vibration measurements the optical
ﬁbre must also be as close to the power cable as possible.
When the power cable is placed oﬀshore the usual placement of the optical ﬁbre is within
the power cable, [65].
The cable technology used in the VikingLink is either MI (Mass Impregnation) insulation
or XLPE (Cross Link Polyethylene) insulation and the oﬀshore cable will be a HVDC
(High Voltage Direct Current) power cable in symmetrical bipole conﬁguration, [1]. In
ﬁgure 6.7 a HVDC bipole conﬁguration is presented together with the optical ﬁbre.
104
==PAGE:115==
6. Solutions
Figure 6.7: Bipole HVDC cable with optical ﬁbre. Figure from [91].
In the power cable conﬁguration seen in ﬁgure 6.7, the optical ﬁbre is placed on the outside
of the polypropylene yarn serving, [71].
Another possible placement of the two poles, in a bipole power cable conﬁguration, is with
the two poles completely separated. In a single HVDC power cable the optical ﬁbre can be
placed as seen in ﬁgure 6.8.
Figure 6.8: Single HVDC cable with optical ﬁbre. Figure from [92].
In ﬁgure 6.8 the optical ﬁbre is placed in the ﬁller between the synthetic tape, or yarn
bedding, and the steal armour, [71].
Most of the damage accounted for in cables, including telecommunication ﬁbres, is from
commercial ﬁshing, which causes 40 % of the faults, [93]. But at greater depths a submarine
power cable would mostly get damaged from internal faults, [71].
Only less than 10 % of the cable faults are due to natural hazards.
105
==PAGE:116==
6. Solutions
Of the two presented power cable conﬁgurations with optical ﬁbres, the single HVDC power
cable conﬁguration, as seen in ﬁgure 6.8, is, from the point of view of the optical ﬁbre and
its properties, the best conﬁguration for fault detection. This is the best conﬁguration
because the optical ﬁbre is closer to the power cable conducting core and insulation. The
power cable conducting core and insulation are where the internal failures happen, [71].
6.11
Cable Monitoring or Fault Localization
Cable monitoring works as constant monitoring of the power cable, by the use of an optical
ﬁbre system, [94].
Fault localization of a power cable, works in the way that an optical ﬁbre solution can be
connected, to the system, in the case where a fault needs to be localised.
OTDRs are often used as hand-held fault localization devices that are brought on site,
after a fault has happened, in order to localize the position of the fault, [52], [56]. The
OTDR system can also be used as a monitoring system, which is presented in [34].
In principle the temperature and strain measurement devices, described throughout this rap-
port, could also be hand-held, but most often they are used as monitoring systems, [58], [59].
The vibration and stain measuring devices are used as both monitoring and fault localization
devices, [62], [66].
106
==PAGE:117==
7
RECOMMENDATIONS
In chapter 6, the solutions to the problem stated in chapter 4 are presented. In this chapter
the solutions are boiled down to the following recommendations.
The basis for the recommendations are all presented in chapter 6.
7.1
Cable Break Sensor
The recommended solution for detecting a break in the power cable, under the assumption
that if the power cable breaks so does the optical ﬁbre, is the product EXFO FTB-7600-E
UHD OTDR, [51].
The recommended ﬁbre used with the OTDR is a SMF ITU-T G.652.
7.2
Temperature Sensor
Neither of the products on the market, nor the state-of-the-art solutions with temperature
measurements are recommended for the VikingLink, as nor can reach the distance of 630
km.
Not taking the VikingLink into account, the OZ Optics DSTS BOTDA [58] is recommended
together with a SMF ITU-T G.652.
7.3
Strain Sensor
For strain sensing together with temperature measurements none of the products on the
market, nor the state-of-the-art solutions are recommended for the VikingLink, as none
can reach the distance of 630 km.
Not taking the VikingLink into account, the same product as for the ﬁbre used as
temperature sensor is recommended. This is the OZ Optics DSTS BOTDA [58]. The
BOTDA is recommended used together with a SMF ITU-T G.652.
7.4
Vibration Sensor
Neither of the products on the market, nor the state-of-the-art solutions for vibration
sensing are recommended for the VikingLink, as none of them can reach the distance of
630 km.
But AP Sensing is indicating some interesting developments in the future, as seen in
appendix B.
Furthermore the solution of Φ-OTDR electrical domain technique with hybrid ampliﬁcation
107
==PAGE:118==
7. Recommendations
has shown to reach 175 km.
For DVS/DAS SMF ITU-T G.652 is recommended.
7.5
Ampliﬁcation
Ampliﬁcation used for the scattering sensing techniques is only recommended for the use of
a coherent set-up, as the C-OTDR presented in 2.10.3. As presented in 6.4.3, the Raman
ampliﬁer is attractive for transmission over long distances.
7.6
Magnetic Field Sensor
As presented in section 6.9, the limitations and issues regarding the implementation of
optical ﬁbres as magnetic ﬁeld sensors in submarine power cables are found to big for
implementation today.
On the basis of this the optical ﬁbre as a magnetic ﬁeld sensor for submarine power cables
is not recommended.
108
==PAGE:119==
8
CONCLUSION
The problem stated in chapter 4, was;
To what degree can optical ﬁbres be used in connection with fault detection in submarine
power cables?
In the following conclusions, summary of contributions and recommendations for work in
the future are presented.
8.1
Conclusions
The problem stated in chapter 4 has been investigated and recommendations for solutions
are presented in chapter 7. It was found that optical ﬁbres can be used in connection with
fault detection in submarine power cables.
On the basis of the proof of concept experiments, as presented in chapter 5, and the solutions
presented in chapter 6, the OTDR is found to be the most promising fault detection method
for VikingLink, under the assumption that if the power cable breaks so does the optical ﬁbre.
8.2
Summary of Contributions
As presented in chapter 3 and stated in section 4.3 neither of the found state of the art
solutions for fault localisation and monitoring of power cables by the use of optical ﬁbres
are able to reach a length of 730 km, as is the case for VikingLink.
In this master project, an investigation of the possible methods for fault localisation and
monitoring of power cables, by the use of optical ﬁbres, of the length of VikingLink are
presented and recommendations are made.
The OTDR was investigated and found a promising solution for fault detection and moni-
toring for the VikingLink.
For the optical ﬁbre as a temperature sensor of power cables, is was found that none of
the products on the market, nor the state of the art solutions can reach the distance of the
VikingLink.
The possibilities of using the optical ﬁbre as a vibration sensor was investigated. Here
interesting development can be expected in the future.
109
==PAGE:120==
8. Conclusion
Investigation of the optical ﬁbre as a magnetic ﬁeld sensor in submarine power cables have
been conducted in section 6.9. Here the limitations of the state of the art for magnetic
ﬁeld sensing with optical ﬁbres was found to big too for such a system to be implemented
in a submarine power cable.
8.3
Future Work
For future work and research ideas are generated throughout the process of working on
the project. These are presented below;
• Cost-Beneﬁt Analysis
This report does not deal with the cost of implementing optical ﬁbres in power cable
systems. Doing a cost-beneﬁt analysis could be a proposed next step in investigating
the implementation of optical ﬁbres in power cable systems. By such an analysis it
could be investigated if it is economically proﬁtable to invest in optical ﬁbres for
fault localization and monitoring of power cables.
• Investigation of Implementation of Optical Communication Link
This report has assumed the optical ﬁbre link in VikingLink as also containing an
optical communication link. This report has not looked into the equipment and
data processing system needed for such a link. A proposed next step would be to
investigate the possibilities, equipment and data processing system for an optical
communication link within the VikingLink.
• Field Experiments
In order to further investigate the possibilities of the fault localization and monitoring
systems recommended in this report, ﬁled experiments are proposed.

A
ELABORATION AND DETAILED
CALCULATIONS
In this appendix elaboration on phenomena, techniques, mechanisms and detailed
calculations from the main report text are presented.
A.1
Geometrical Description of Optical Fibres
A.1.1
Step-Index Fibre
The maximum angle that the incident ray can make with the ﬁbre axis, and still remain
conﬁned inside the core, is found to be
N.A = n0sinθi = n1[1 −(n2/n1)2]1/2 = (n2
1 −n2
2)1/2
(A.1)
called the numerical aperture, [3].
For ﬁbres with n1 ≈n2 the numerical aperture can be approximated by N.A = n1(2∆)1/2
with ∆≈(n1 −n2)/n1.
A.1.2
Graded-Index Fibre
In a graded-index ﬁbre the refractive index in the core is not constant, but decreases
gradually from its maximum value n1 at the center of the core, to its minimum value n2
at the core-cladding interface, as seen in ﬁgure A.1.
Graded-index ﬁbres can be designed to a have a nearly quadratic decrease and can be
described by the parameter a, which determines the index proﬁle as
n(r) =



n1[1 −∆(r/a)a];
r < a
n1(1 −∆) = n2;
r ≤a
(A.2)
with a as the core radius and r as the distance from the center of the ﬁbre. For a high a
value the graded-index ﬁbre becomes step-index-like.
Figure A.2 shows schematically the paths for three diﬀerent rays. Here it is seen that the
ray propagating along the graded-index ﬁbre axis takes the shortest path, but travels most
slowly as the refractive index is largest along this path. Oblique rays have a large part of
their path in a medium of lower refractive index, where they travel faster. It is therefore
possible for all rays, by a suitable choice of the refractive-index proﬁle, to arrive together
at the ﬁbre output.
119
==PAGE:130==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.1: Cross section and refractive-index proﬁle for a graded-index ﬁbre.
Figure from [3].
Figure A.2: Ray trajectories in a graded-index ﬁbre, ﬁgure from [4].
A.2
Wave Propagation
A.2.1
Maxwell’s Equations
Maxwell’s equations in a dielectric, being an electrical insulator that can be polarized by
an applied electric ﬁeld, take the form;
∇· B =0
(A.3)
∇· E =0
(A.4)
∇× H =ϵµ∂E
∂t
(A.5)
∇× E = −µ∂H
∂t
(A.6)
with the electric ﬁeld as E and the magnetic ﬁeld as B. For simple homogeneous materials
in moderate ﬁelds the linear ﬁeld relations are D = ϵE and B = µH. The displacement
ﬁeld is D and the magnetic ﬁeld strength is H.
The connection to free space or vacuum permittivity ϵ0 is ϵ = ϵ0(1 + χ) and vacuum
120
==PAGE:131==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
permeability µ0 is µ = µ0(1 + χm).
The susceptibilities χ and χm describes how easy it is to polarize or magnetize the material.
The two constants, vacuum permittivity ϵ0 and vacuum permeability µ0, can be determined
experimentally. Their values are ϵ0 = 8.85 · 10−12 F/m and µ0 = 4π · 10−7 H/m, [3]. The
units can also be expressed as [F/m] = [As/(Vm)] and [H/m] = [N/A2].
A.2.2
Fibre Modes
A mode is a solution to the wave equation, as presented in equation 2.1.
For the step-index ﬁbre it is advantageous to use the cylindrical symmetry, therefore the
wave equation is written in cylindrical coordinates, φ, r and z, as
∇2ψ = ∂2ψ

∂r + 1

∂φ2 + ∂2ψ
∂z2 = n(r)2

∂2ψ
∂t
(A.7)
with φ as the angle, r as the the radius coordinate and z running along the length of the
ﬁbre. The solution of the wave equation is ψ(r, φ, z, t) = ψ(r, φ)exp[i(ωt −βz)].
The frequency is found from ∂/∂t2 = −ω2ψ and the propagation constant from
∂/∂z2 = −βψ.
The wave equation can be rewritten to

The radius of the core is a, so n(r) is deﬁned as n(r) = n1 for 0 < r < a in the core and
n(r) = n2 for r > a for the cladding assuming it continues to inﬁnity.

A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
The separated radial equation can be rewritten into two equations, one concerning the
core and one for cladding
r2 ∂2R
∂r2 + r∂R
∂r + [(Ur)2 −l2]R =0
0 < r < a
(A.13)
r2 ∂2R

r > a
(A.14)
The general solution in the core and the cladding regions can be written as
R(r) =
n AJm(Ur) + A′Ym(Ur);
r ≤a
CKm(Wr) + CIm(Wr);
r > a
(A.15)
where A, A′, C and C′ are constants and Jm, Ym, Km and Im are diﬀerent kinds of Bessel
functions.
The boundary conditions states that the optical ﬁeld for a guided mode should be ﬁnite at
r = 0 and decay to zero at r = ∞.
Because Ym(Ur) has a singularity at r = 0, R(r) can remain ﬁnite if only A′ = 0. Jm is
the Bessel functions of ﬁrst kind and for the core solutions they are bounded oscillatory
functions.
Similarly for the cladding, will only R(r) vanish at inﬁnity if C′ = 0. Km are the modiﬁed
Bessel functions of the second kind and for the cladding solution they show a roughly
exponential decay. Plots of the two lowest-order J and K functions are shown in ﬁgure
A.3.
Figure A.3: Plots of the two lowest-order Bessel functions of the ﬁrst kind (left)
and modiﬁed Bessel functions of the second kind (right), ﬁgure from [12].
The solution is an oscillatory cosine-like function in the core that should be matched to an
exponentially decaying function in the cladding.
A mode is determined by its propagation constant β = neffk0 and is no longer guided
when neff ≤n2. This is due to the fact that the optical ﬁeld of guided modes decay
exponentially inside the cladding layer as the modiﬁed Bessel function
Km(Wr) = (π/2Wr)1/2exp(−Wr)
Wr ≫1
(A.16)

Figure A.4: Eﬀective index of various LPml modes as functions of V for a
step-index ﬁber with n2 = 1.444 , and n1 = 1.45, graph from [12].
As seen in ﬁgure A.4, a ﬁbre with a large value of V supports many modes. Thus the
Bessel function can have many zero points. The modes of circular-symmetric ﬁbres can be
indexed according to the so-called LPml scheme. LP stands for ’Linearly Polarized’, and
refers to the fact that the the polarization is assumed uniform along one axis of the ﬁbre,
[12]. An LPml mode becomes increasingly more conﬁned to the core with increasing V .
Below a certain value all modes, except the fundamental LP01 mode, reach cutoﬀ. A mode
reaches cutoﬀwhen W = 0. Since Ua = V when W = 0 the cutoﬀcondition for modes is
given by J0(V ) = 0. The smallest value of V for which J0(V ) = 0 is 2.405. At this value
only the fundamental mode is supported in the ﬁbre and the ﬁbre type only supporting
the fundamental mode is known as a singlemode ﬁbre (SMF).

A.3
Dispersion
A.3.1
Group-Velocity Dispersion
The phase of a monochromatic wave travelling at phase vp can be expressed as vp = ω/β,
with ω as the frequency and β as the propagation constant. Superposition of monochromatic
pulses will form a group, and if the group has a constant amplitude, the group will propagate
with the group-velocity given by

Figure A.8: Propagation constant β versus ω in a dispersive medium with

straight line given by the larger n1. The solid line in ﬁgure A.8 mimics the behaviour of
the fundamental mode in a singlemode ﬁbre, where the mode is mostly located in the
cladding, n2, for low frequencies and mostly in the core, n1, for high frequencies. In such a

waveguide dispersion as D = DM + DW .
A.3.2
Material Dispersion
Material dispersion is related to the characteristic resonance frequency at which a material
absorbs electromagnetic radiation, [3]. Material dispersion occurs as the refractive index
of the ﬁbre material, often silica, changes the optical frequency, ω. The frequencies far
from the resonance frequencies can be approximated to the Sellmeier equation, [3]

the normal dispersion and the wavelength region above is the anomalous dispersion.
A.3.3
Waveguide Dispersion
As described in subsection A.3.1 the waveguide dispersion depends on the V -parameter of
the ﬁbre. The waveguide dispersion is negative in the wavelength range from 0 µm to 1.6
µm, where the material dispersion is negative for wavelengths below λZD and becomes
positive above that. The material dispersion, DM, the waveguide dispersion, DW and the
total dispersion, D versus wavelength for a standard singlemode function is presented in
ﬁgure A.11.
The waveguide distribution shifts the zero-dispersion wavelength by 30-40 nm, so the total
dispersion is zero near 1.31 µm. The waveguide dispersion also reduces the total dispersion
from its material value, DM in the wavelength range from 1.3-1.6 µm. From a dispersion
point of view this makes transmission at 1.3 µm very attractive. Unfortunately is the
attenuation at this wavelength signiﬁcantly larger than at 1.55 µm.
130
==PAGE:141==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.11: Total dispersion D and relative contributions of material dispersion
DM and waveguide dispersion Dm for a standard singlemode ﬁbre. The zero-
dispersion wavelength shifts to a higher value due to the waveguide contribution.
Figure from [13].
Figure A.12: Typical wavelength dependence for the dispersion parameter D for
standard, dispersion-shifted, and dispersion-ﬂattened ﬁbres, ﬁgure from [13].
As the wavelength contribution to dispersion depends on ﬁbre parameters, such as the
core radius a and the refractive indices, it is possible to design a ﬁbre in such a way that
λZD is shifted into the vicinity of 1.55 µm. This ﬁbre type is called dispersion-shifted
ﬁbres (DSF). Furthermore it is also possible to tailor the waveguide contribution so that
the total dispersion is relatively small over a wide wavelength range. This type of ﬁbre is
called dispersion-ﬂattened ﬁbres (DFF). Figure A.12 shows a standard singlemode ﬁber
dispersion as function of wavelength together with the dispersion for a dispersion-shifted
131
==PAGE:142==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
ﬁbre, where the zero-dispersion wavelength has been shifted to around 1.5 µm. In the
ﬁgure the dispersion for a dispersion-ﬂattened ﬁbre is also shown.
A.3.4
Higher-Order Dispersion
All the dispersive eﬀect does not disappear, despite operation at the zero-dispersion
wavelength λZD where D = 0. Optical pulses will still experience broadening due to higher-
order dispersive eﬀects. This is noticed by the fact the D cannot be zero at all wavelengths
contained in the pulse spectrum centered at λZD. Higher-order dispersive eﬀects are
governed by the dispersion slope of S = dD/dλ, with S as the diﬀerential-dispersion
parameter. By the use of equation A.26 it can be expressed as
S = dD

(A.52)
where β3 = dβ2/dω = d3β/dω is the third-order dispersion parameter. At λ = λZD, β2 = 0
and S is proportional to β3.
A.3.5
Polarization Mode Dispersion (PMD)
As presented in subsection 2.2.2 Fibre Birefringence small derivations in the shape of
the cylindrical symmetry of the ﬁbre leads to birefringence, due to diﬀerent mode indices
associated with the orthogonally polarized components of the fundamental ﬁbre mode. If
the pulse excites both polarizations, it becomes broader, as the the polarization components
separate along the ﬁbre, due to their diﬀerent velocities. This phenomenon is presented in
ﬁgure A.13
Figure A.13:
Polarization mode dispersion.
Diﬀerent mode indicies for
orthogonal polarized components of the fundamental ﬁbre mode will, if the optical
pulse excites both polarizations, become broader. Figure from [4].
Polarization-maintaining ﬁbres have a constant birefringence and the pulse broadening
can be estimated from the time delay between the two polarization components during
propagation of the pulse. For a ﬁbre of length L the time delay ∆T is given by
∆T =
 L
vgx
−L
vgy

(A.53)
132
==PAGE:143==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
with vg as the group velocity and the subscripts x and y identifying the two orthogonally
polarized modes. The quantity ∆T/L is a measure of PMD.
For conventional ﬁbres the birefringence varies randomly along the length of the ﬁbre.
The pulse broadening induced by random changes in the birefringence is referred to as
PMD-induced pulse broadening, [4]. It is characterized by the root-mean-square (RMS)
value of ∆T, which is obtained after averaging over random birefringence changes. The
variance, σ2

approximated as σt ≈(∆β1)√2lcL = Dp

PMD Link Design Value (PMDQ)
In order to understand the statistical deﬁnition of PMDQ consider a ﬁbre link consisting
of M equal length ﬁbre sections, as seen in ﬁgure A.14.
Figure A.14: Schematic illustration of a ﬁbre link made with M ﬁbre sections
of the same length, ﬁgure from [10].
Each ﬁbre section of the link has its own PMD value and from this the PMD link can be
calculated for the entire link, using
PMDLINK(M) =
1
M1/2 [PMD2
1 + PMD2
2 + ... + PMD2
M]1/2
(A.55)
The process of selecting ﬁbres from a ﬁbre manufactures inventory is random and therefore
the PMD link coeﬃcient becomes a random value. The quality of the distribution of PMD
can be controlled by the requirement that the probability of the PMD link must not be
higher than a certain threshold. The number of ﬁbre sections chosen for the reference link
is designated by letter M and the the probability of exceeding a threshold value PMDQ is
denoted as Q.

A.4
Losses
A.4.1
Attenuation Coeﬃcient
It is custom to express the attenuation coeﬃcient in dB/km, by using the relation
α(dB/km) = −10

(A.56)
The ﬁbre loss depends on the transmitted wavelength. In ﬁgure A.15 the loss spectrum
α(λ) of a singlemode ﬁbre made in 1979 is presented. The lowest region of loss is at the
wavelength region near 1550 nm of only 0.2 dB/km.
Figure A.15:
Spectrum of loss in a singlemode ﬁber produced in 1979.

In ﬁgure A.15 several fundamental loss mechanisms are also presented and they also
contribute to the overall loss. The two most important among them are material absorption
and Rayleigh scattering.
A.4.2
Material Absorption
Material absorption can be divided into two categories, intrinsic absorption and extrinsic
absorption.
Intrinsic Absorption
Intrinsic absorption losses correspond to the absorption caused by the material used to
make ﬁbres, usually fused silica. Any material absorbs a speciﬁc wavelength corresponding
to the electronic and vibrational resonances within the speciﬁc molecules, [3]. For silica
134
==PAGE:145==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
molecules (SiO2) the electronic resonance occurs in the ultraviolet region, where λ < 0.4 µm
and the vibrational resonances occur in the infrared region of λ > 7 µm. In ﬁgure A.15
the intrinsic material absorption for silica is shown. Here it is seen that in the wavelength
range from 0.8 µm to 1.6 µm the intrinsic absorption is less than 0.1 dB/km and it is less
than 0.03 dB/km in the wavelength region of 1.3 µm to 1.6 µm.
Extrinsic Absorption
Extrinsic absorption is related to the loss caused by the impurities within silica. The main
source of extrinsic absorption is from the presence of water vapors. The hydroxide (OH)
ion has a vibrational resonance near 2.73 µm, [6]. It creates absorption at 1.39 µm, 1.24
µm and 0.95 µm. Three spectral peaks occurs near these wavelengths and are seen in
ﬁgure A.15. These peaks are caused by the presence of residual water vapor in silica. A
concentration of 1 ppm (part per million) can cause a loss of approximately 50 dB/km at
1.39 µm. In dry ﬁbres the OH concentration level is reduced to such a low level that the
1.39 µm peak almost disappears, [3]. Figure A.16 shows the loss and dispersion of a dry
ﬁbre and a conventional ﬁbre.
Figure A.16: Loss and dispersion of a conventional ﬁbre and a dry ﬁbre, from
[3].
A.5
Scattering
A.5.1
Brillouin Scattering
For an oscillating electrical ﬁeld at the pump frequency Ωp, the process of the Brillouin
scattering generates an acoustic wave at a frequency Ω. Spontaneous Brillouin scattering
can be viewed as scattering of the pump wave from the acoustic wave, which will result
in the creation of a new scattering wave ΩS. The scattering process must be subjected
to energy and momentum conservation. The energy conservation is satisﬁed by the fact
that the Stokes shift Ωequals the diﬀerence in frequencies of the pump and the scattered
frequency, as ωp −ωs. The conservation of momentum requires that the wavevector of the
acoustic wave equals the diﬀerence in wavevector of the pump and the scattered wavevector,
as kA = kp −kS. From this the following relation is revealed
135
==PAGE:146==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
|kA|2 =|kp|2 + |kS|2 −2|kp||kS|cosθ ≈2|kp|2[1 −cosθ]
(A.57)
=2|kk|22sin2θ
2

(A.58)
|kA| =2|kp|sin2θ
2

(A.59)
where θ is the angle between the pump and scattered waves.
Using the relation |kA| = Ω/νA, where νA is the velocity of the acoustic wave, this condition
determines the acoustic frequency as
Ω= |kA|νA = 2νA|kp|sin2θ
2

(A.60)

the backward direction as θ = π. Thus determining that spontaneous Brillouin scatter
only backscatter with a frequency shift of ΩB = 2νA|kp|. Using equation A.60 at θ = π
and kp = 2πneff/λp the Brillouin frequency shift is
vB = ΩB

GHz at λp = 1.55 µm.
