black vertical arrow respresents the pump and the red vertical arrow represent the
Brillouin shift of ﬁbre (c). Figure from [4].
Fibre (a) has a core of nearly pure silica with a germania concentration of about 0.3 %
per mole and has a Brilluin shift of vB = 11.25 GHz. The Brilluin shift is reduced for
the ﬁbres labelled (b) and (c) as they have a higher germania concentration in the ﬁbre
core. The double-peak structure for ﬁbre (b) is due to inhomogeneous distribution of the
germania in the core of the ﬁbre.
The gain bandwidth is diﬀerent for each ﬁbre as the core diameter can vary along the
length of the ﬁbre. Typical values of the gain bandwidth are ∆vB = 50 −100 MHz for
pump wavelengths near 1.55 µm.

The coupled equations for the forward stimulated Raman scattering (SRS) are given as
dIp

(A.65)

(A.66)
with gR as the SRS gain, Ip as the intensity of the pump, Is as the intensity of the SRS

For the backward stimulated Raman scattering the coupled equations are identical to the

dIp

(A.67)
−dIs

(A.68)

The Raman shift is given as ΩR = ωp −ωs. The maximum gain occurs at ΩR = 13 THz
and the value of the peak of the gain gR for a wavelength of 1550 nm is gR = 6 · 10−13

A.6
Fibre Types
A.6.1
Multimode Fibre with a 50-Micron Core (ITU-T G.651)
The ITU-T G.651 is an MMF with a 50 µm nominal core diameter and a 125 µm nominal
cladding diameter with a graded refractive index. The attenuation parameter for G.651
ﬁbre is typically 0.8 dB/km at 1310 nm. The main application for ITU-T G.651 ﬁbre is
138
==PAGE:149==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
for short-reach optical transmission systems. The ﬁbre is optimized for use in the 1300 nm
band. It can also operate in the 850 nm band.
A.6.2
Low Water Peak Non-Dispersion Shifted Fibre (ITU-T G.652.C)
The standard ITU-T G.652 SMFs are not optimized for wavelength-division multiplexing
(WDM) applications due to the high attenuation around the water peak region. The
category of ITU G.652.C ﬁbres oﬀer low attenuation around the OH peaks.
The
G.652.C ﬁbre is optimized for networks where transmission occurs across a broad range
of wavelengths from 1285 nm to 1625 nm. Although the G.652.C ﬁbres oﬀer excellent
capabilities for shorter, unampliﬁed metro and access networks, they do not fully address
the needs for 1550 nm transmissions. An example of this type of ﬁbre is Corning SMF-28e.
A.6.3
1550 nm Loss Minimized Fibre (ITU-T G.654)
The ITU-T G.654 ﬁbre is optimized for operation in the 1500 nm to 1600 nm region, [11].
The ﬁbre has a low loss in the 1550 nm band. The low loss is achieved by the use of a pure
silica core. ITU-T G.654 ﬁbres can handle high power levels and have a large core area.
The ITU G.654 ﬁbre has been designed for extended long-haul submarine applications.
Two categories, A and B, exist and they are distinguished in the PMDQ link design value
speciﬁcation and the geometrical parameters and the macrobending loss speciﬁcations,
[10].
A.6.4
Nonzero Dispersion Shifted Fibre (ITU-T G.655)
The use of nonzero dispersion-shifted ﬁbre (NZDSF) can correct for nonlinear characteris-
tics. NZDSF overcomes these eﬀects by moving the zero-dispersion wavelength outside the
1550 nm operating window. The NZDSF has an absolute value of the chromatic dispersion
coeﬃcient that is greater than some non-zero values throughout the wavelength range from
1530 nm to 1565 nm, [10]. This small and ﬁnite amount of chromatic dispersion minimizes
nonlinear eﬀects, such as four-wave mixing (FWM), self-phase modulation (SPM), and
cross-phase modulation (XPM), which are particularly harmful in dense wavelength-division
multiplexed (DWDM) systems.
There are two ﬁbre families called nonzero dispersion (NZD+ and NZD–), where the
zero-dispersion value falls before and after the 1550 nm wavelength, respectively. The
attenuation parameter for G.655 ﬁbre is typically 0.2 dB/km at 1550 nm, and the PMD
parameter is less than 0.1 ps/ km. The Corning LEAF ﬁbre is an example of a G.655 ﬁbre.
A.6.5
Nonzero Dispersion for Wideband Optical Transport (ITU-T
G.656)
The G.656 ﬁbre is a NZDSF developed for optimized dispersion characteristics in high-
capacity, long-haul networks. This type of ﬁbre can be utilized for both coarse wavelength
division multiplexing (CWDM) and DWDM systems throughout the wavelength region
between 1460 nm and 1625 nm. The chromatic dispersion of the ﬁbre reduces the growth
139
==PAGE:150==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
of non-linear eﬀects that are harmful in DWDM systems over a wider wavelength range
than the ITU-T G.655 ﬁbre.
A.6.6
Bend Insensitive Fibre (ITU-T G.657)
The bend insensitive ﬁbre reduces the bend sensitivity of the ﬁbre, as compared to the
G.652 singlemode ﬁbre. Two categories of the bend insensitive ﬁbre exist.
Category A ﬁbres are suitable to be used throughout the 1260 nm to 1625 nm range.
Category B ﬁbres are suitable for transmission at 1310 nm, 1550 nm and 1625 nm for
restricted distances that are associated with in-building transport of signals. The improved
macrobending behaviour of ITU-T G.657 ﬁbres can be used in broadband optical access
networks, small volume ﬁbre management systems and low radius mounting, [10].
A.6.7
Dispersion Flattened Fibre (DFF)
PFFs have a relatively constant group delay dispersion over a wavelength range, which
means they have a low higher order dispersion, [95]. Dispersion is presented in section A.3.
A.7
Transmitters
A.7.1
The Laser
The term LASER is an acronym for Light Ampliﬁcation by Stimulated Emission of
Radiation. It consists of an energy source (pump source in ﬁgure A.20) and a gain material
(laser medium in ﬁgure A.20) placed in an optical resonator constructed by two mirrors,
where one is a highly reﬂective mirror and the other is a partially reﬂective mirror.
The laser is an optical oscillator. It consists of a resonant optical ampliﬁer, whose output
is feedback into the the input. The oscillation process can be initiated by the presence
of even a small amount of noise with frequency components lying within the bandwidth
of the ampliﬁer. This input is ampliﬁed and the output is fed back to the input, where
it again undergoes further ampliﬁcation. This process continues until a large output is
produced. In order for the oscillation to occur the ampliﬁer gain must be larger than the
loss in in the feedback system. The increase of the output is limited by the saturation of
the ampliﬁer gain and the system reaches a steady state where the gain equals the loss.
The ampliﬁcation occurs when a photon, from the pump, interacts with the laser medium,
thus when light and matter interact.
140
==PAGE:151==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.20: Schematic drawing of a typical laser, showing the pump source,
the laser medium and the optical resonator. Figure from [96].
A.7.2
Energy Levels
Each atom or molecule in a collection constantly undergoes random transitions between
its energy levels. These transitions are characterized by the rules of statistical physics, [6].
A.7.3
Boltzmann Distribution
When considering a collection of atoms or molecules, each atom is in one of its allowed
energy levels, E1, E2, . . . If the system is in thermal equilibrium at temperature T, meaning
the motion the atoms reach a steady state, the probability of an arbitrary atom begin in
an energy level is given by the Boltzmann distribution:
P(Em) ∝exp(−Em/kT)
m = 1, 2, 3, ...
(A.69)
where k is the Boltzmann constant. The coeﬃcient of proportionality is chosen such that
the expression can be normalized to
X
m
P(Em) = 1
(A.70)
The occupation probability P(Em) is an exponentially decreasing function of Em as seen
in ﬁgure A.21.
Considering a large number of atoms N, the number of atoms occupying level Em is Nm
and the Nm/N ≈P(Em).
If N1 atoms occupy level 1 and N2 atoms occupy a higher level 2, the population is on
average
N2
N1
= exp

−E2 −E1
kT

(A.71)
At T = 0 K all atoms are in the lowest energy level, their ground state. At equilibrium
conditions, the average population of a given energy level is always greater that that of a
141
==PAGE:152==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.21: The Boltzmann distribution provides the probability that energy
level Em of an arbitrary atom is occupied, which is an exponentially decreasing
function of Em. Figure from [6].
higher lying level. At non-equilibrium conditions a higher lying energy level can have a
greater average population than a lower lying energy level. This is population inversion
and provides the basis for laser conditions.
A.7.4
Fermi-Dirac Distribution
Quantum systems with overlapping wavefunction, as multielectron atoms and semiconduc-
tors, are all governed by Paulis exclusion principle.
The number of electrons Nm in a state is either 0 or 1. The probability of occupancy of a
state of energy E is described by the Fermi-Dirac distribution, sometimes refered to as the
Fermi function
f(E) =
1
exp[(E −Ef)/kT] + 1
(A.72)
where Ef is the Fermi energy. The maximum value of E is where the state of energy
level is occupied. By a value of 1/2 the Fermi energy E = Ef. f(E) is a distribution of
probabilities for diﬀerent values of E, between 0 and 1. When E ≫Ef and E ≪kT, the
Fermi-Dirac distribution behaves as the Boltzmann probability distribution
P(E) ∝exp(−E/kt)
(A.73)
The Fermi-Dirac and Boltzmann distributions are compared in ﬁgure A.22.
A.7.5
Light and Matter
A photon may interact with an atom if its energy matches the diﬀerence between two
atomic energy levels.
An atom may emit (create) or absorb (annihilate) a photon by undergoing downward or
upward transitions between its energy levels, conserving the energy in the process. The
laws that govern these fundamental processes are absorption, spontaneous emission and
stimulated emission, as presented in ﬁgure A.23.
142
==PAGE:153==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.22:
The Fermi-Dirac distribution f(E) is approximated by the
Boltzmann distribution P(E) when E ≫Ef. Figure from [6].
Figure A.23: Three fundamental processes occurring between the two energy
states of an atom, absorption, spontaneous emission and stimulated emission,
ﬁgure from [4].
The three fundamental interactions processes can also be viewed from an energy-level point
of view, by considering the energy levels E1 and E2 of an atom. If the photon energy hν
of an incident light of frequency ν, is equal to the energy diﬀerence between the two levels,
Eg = E2 −E1, then the photons of this energy matches the atomic energy-level diﬀerence.
This leads to the three forms of interaction previously mentioned;
• Absorption
If the atom is initially in the lower energy level, the photon may be annihilated and,
at the same time, the atom will raise to the upper energy level.
• Spontaneous Emission
If the atom is initially in the upper energy level it may decay spontaneously to the
lower energy level and release its energy in the form of a photon. This process is the
underlying phenomenon in light-emitting diodes.
• Stimulated Emission
If the atom is in the upper energy level, the atom may be induced to emit another
photon. The presence of a photon, with the speciﬁc frequency and propagating
direction, in the cavity can stimulate the emission of a duplicate ("clone") photon with
precisely the same characteristics as the original photon. This photon ampliﬁcation
is the underlying phenomenon in laser ampliﬁers and laser diodes.
143
==PAGE:154==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
A.7.6
Semiconductor Optics
A semiconductor is a crystalline or amorphous solid, whose electrical conductivity is
typically intermediate between that of a metal and that of an insulator, [6].
In a semiconductor the atoms are arranged in a crystal lattice. The individual energy levels
associated with a single atom merge and form energy bands. The relevant bands are the
outer bands constructing the energy band gap or the energy diﬀerence between the energy
levels, the conduction and the valence band. The conduction band is the topmost band
ﬁlled with electrons and the valence band is the ﬁrst band ﬁlled with holes or it is nearly
empty. The holes are the empty states left behind in the valence band if the electrons are
raised to the conduction band. The bands are represented in ﬁgure A.24, where k = p/ℏis
the wavevector of an electron with momentum p and energy E.
Figure A.24: Conduction and valence bands of a semiconductor. The electron in
the conduction band and the holes in the valence band recombine to emit photons,
ﬁgure from [3].
Near the bottom of the conduction band, the E −k relation can be approximated by a
parabola as
E = Ec + ℏ2k2
2mc
(A.74)
where Ec is the energy in the bottom of the conduction band and mc is the electron
(conduction-band) eﬀective mass.
Similarly, near the top of the valence band, the E −k relation is approximated by a
parabola as
E = Ev −ℏ2k2
2mv
(A.75)
where Ev = Ec −Eg is the energy at the top of the valence band, Eg is the energy of the
bandgap in the semiconductor and mv is the hole (valence-band) eﬀective mass.
144
==PAGE:155==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Light is emitted if an electron near the bottom of the conduction band recombines with a
hole in the valence band. The emitted photon from the recombination process will have an
energy of Eg ≈hν. The three interaction processes of absorption, spontaneous emission
and stimulated emission, can be described using the E −k-diagram as seen ﬁgure A.25.
The absorption process is used in photodetectors, the spontaneous emission process is used
in LEDs and the stimulated emission process is used in laser diodes and semiconductor
optical ampliﬁers (SOA).
Figure A.25: (a) The absorption of a photon results in the generation of an
electron-hole pair. This process is used in the photodetection of light. (b) The
recombination of an electron-hole pair results in the spontaneous emission of a
photon. Light-emitting diodes (LEDs) operate on this basis. (c) Electron-hole
recombination can be induced by a photon. The result is the stimulated emission
of an identical photon. This is the underlying process responsible for the operation
of semiconductor laser diodes. Figure from [6].
The conditions under which absorption and emission, meaning the downward transitions
and thereby also the conditions for optical transitions in semiconductors, can take place
are summarized as follows,
• Conservation of Energy
The absorption and emission of a photon of energy hν requires that the energies of
the two states involved in the interaction, that being E1 and E2 in the valence and
conduction band, respectively, are separated by hν. Therefore for photon emission to
occur by electron hole recombination, an electron occupying an energy level E2 must
interact with a hole occupying an energy level E1, so that the energy is conserved as
E2 −E1 = hν
(A.76)
• Conservation of Momentum
Momentum must also be conserved, so that p2 −p1 = hν/c = h/λ or k2 −k1 = 2π/λ.
The momenta of the electron and the hole participating in the interaction must be
approximately equal, [6]. This is known as the k-selecting rule, where k2 ≈k1. The
transitions that obey this rule are represented in the E −k-diagram in ﬁgure A.24
and ﬁgure A.25 as vertical lines, which indicates that the change in k is negligible on
the scales of the diagrams.
145
==PAGE:156==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
• Energies and Momenta of the Electron and Hole with Which a Photon Interacts
Conservation of energy and momentum requires that a photon of frequency ν
interacts with electrons and holes of speciﬁc energies and momenta determined
by the semiconductor E −k relation. Using equation A.74 and A.75 and writing
Ec −Ev = Eg the relation for the energy of the direct-bandgap semiconductor can
be written as
E2 −E1 = ℏ2k2
2mv
+ Eg + ℏ2k2

= hν
(A.77)
where

It is seen that there is a one-to-one correspondence between E2 and ν, presented in

Figure A.26: The density of states with which a photon of energy hν interacts
increases with hν −Eg. Figure from [6].
• Photon Emission Is Unlikely in an Indirect-Bandgap Semiconductor
Electron-hole recombination is unlikely to happen in an indirect-bandgap
semiconductor. This is due to the fact that the transition from near the bottom of
the conduction band (where the electrons are most likely to reside) to the top of
the valence band (where the holes are most likely to reside) requires an exchange of
momentum that the emitted photon cannot meet. Momentum can be conserved by
the help of phonons. Phonons can carry large momenta but do typically have small
energies and therefore they appear horizontal on the E −k diagram, as seen in ﬁgure
A.27.
Figure A.27: Photon emission in an indirect-bandgap semiconductor. The
recombination of an electron near the bottom of the conduction band with a hole
near the top of the valence band requires exchange of both energy and momentum.
The energy may be carried oﬀby a photon, but one or more phonons are required
to conserve momentum. This multiparticle interaction is unlikely. Figure from
[6].
This results in the momentum being conserved, but the k-selection rule being violated.
The phonon-assisted emission involves participation of three bodies; the electron, the
photon and the phonon, and thus the probability of this to occur is quite low, [6].
147
==PAGE:158==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
• Photon Absorption Is Likely in an Indirect-Bandgap Semiconductor
Photon absorption in an indirect-bandgap semiconductor requires energy and
momentum conservation and is achieved by means of a two-step process, as seen in
ﬁgure A.28. The electron is excited to a high energy level within the conduction band
by a k-conserving vertical transition. Then it is quickly relaxed to the bottom of the
conduction band by a thermalization process, where its momentum is transferred to
phonons. The generated hole behaves in the same way. As this process occurs in
sequences, it does not require the presence of three bodies at the same time, and it
is thus not unlikely.
Figure A.28: Photon absorption in an indirect-bandgap semiconductor by means
of vertical (k-conserving) transition. The photon generates an excited electron in
the conduction band and leaves a hole behind in the valence band. The electron
and hole then undergo a fast transition to the lowest and highest possible levels in
the conduction and valence bands, respectively. Thereby releasing their energy in
the form of phonons. Figure from [6].
A.7.7
Occupancy Probabilities
The conditions for occupancies are summarized as follows;
• Emission
The condition for emission is given as a conduction band state of energy E2 is ﬁlled
with an electron and a valence band state of energy E1 is empty and thus ﬁlled with
a hole.
The probability fe(ν) that the emission condition is satisﬁed, for a photon of energy
hν, is the product of the probability that the upper state is ﬁlled and the probability
the lower state is empty.
The probability is determined by the appropriate Fermi functions fc(E) and
fv(E) associated with the conduction and valence bands of a semiconductor in
quasi-equilibrium (Efc ̸= Efv), as introduced in subsection 2.8.1 Fermi-Dirac
Distribution, given as
fe(ν) = fc(E2)[1 −fv(E1)]
(A.86)
and presented in ﬁgure A.29.
148
==PAGE:159==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
• Absorption
The condition for absorption is that a conduction band state of energy E2 is empty
and a valence band state of energy E1 is ﬁlled.
Similarly to the emission condition, the probability fa(ν) that the absorption condition
is satisﬁed is
fa(ν) = [1 −fc(E2)]fv(E1)
(A.87)
and presented in ﬁgure A.29.
Figure A.29: The probability that a particular conduction band energy level E
is occupied by an electron is fe(E), a Fermi function with Fermi level Efc. The
probability that a valence band energy level E is occupied by a hole is 1 −fv(E),
where fv(E) is a Fermi function with Fermi level Efv. Figure from [6].
• Net Emission
The net emission is also known as the Fermi inversion factor and is given by
fg(ν) ≡fe(ν) −fa(ν) = fc(E2) −fv(E1)
(A.88)
From equation A.88 the condition for net emission can be derived as
Efc −Efv > hν
(A.89)
A.7.8
Junctions
Juxtapositions of diﬀerent doped regions of a single semiconductor material are known
as homojunctions.
Junctions of diﬀerent semiconductor materials are known as
heterojunctions.
A.7.9
The p −n Junction
A p −n junction is a homojunction between a p-type and a n-type semiconductor. The
p-type has an abundance of holes and thus a positive charge. The n-type has an abundance
of electrons and thus a negative charge. The energy levels and carrier concentration for a
p-type and a n-type semiconductor before contact is presented in ﬁgure A.30.
149
==PAGE:160==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.30: Energy levels and carrier concentrations for a p-type and an n-type
semiconductor before contact. Figure from [6].
The p −n junction works as a diode and can, among others, serve as a light-emitting diode
(LED), a laser diode or a photodiode (PD).
A p −n junction consists of a p-type and a n-type section made of the same semiconductor
materials in contact.
When the two regions are brought into contact, as seen in ﬁgure A.31, the following events
will take place;
Figure A.31: A p−n junction in thermal equilibrium at T >0° K. The depletion-
layer, energy-band diagram and concentrations (on a logarithmic scale) of mobile
electrons, n(x) and holes h(x) are shown as functions of position x. The built-in
potential diﬀerence V0 corresponds to an energy eV0, where e is the magnitude of
the electron charge. Figure from [6].
• The electrons and holes from areas of high concentration will move toward areas
150
==PAGE:161==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
of low concentration. Meaning the electrons will diﬀuse from the n-region into
the p-region, and will leave positively charged ionized donor atoms behind. In the
p-region the electrons will recombine with the abundant holes. In the same way
the holes will diﬀuse from the p-region into the n-region, leaving behind negatively
charged ionized acceptor atoms. In the n-region the holes will recombine with the
abundant mobile electrons. This diﬀusion process will not continue indeﬁnitely and
it causes a disruption of the charge in the two regions.
• The narrow region on both sides of the junction becomes nearly depleted of mobile
charge carriers. This region is known as the depletion layer. The depletion layer
only contains the ﬁxed charges (positive ions on the n-side and negative ions on
the p-side). The thickness of the depletion layer is inversely proportional to the
concentration of dopants in the region.
• The ﬁxed charges create an electric ﬁeld in the depletion layer that points from the
n-side toward the p-side of the junction. This build-in ﬁeld will prevent the diﬀusion
of further mobile carriers through the junction region.
• An equilibrium condition is established which will result in a built-in potential
diﬀerence V0 between the two sides of the depletion layer, with the n-side exhibiting
a higher potential than the p-side.
• The built-in potential provides a lower potential energy for an electron on the n-side
as compared to the p-side. As a results, the energy bands will bend, as seen in ﬁgure
A.31.
• No net current will ﬂow across the junction.
The currents associated with the
diﬀusion and built-in ﬁeld cancel for both the electrons and the holes.
A.7.10
The Biased p −n Junction
By applying an external potential the potential diﬀerence between the p- and the n-regions
will be altered. This will in turn modify the ﬂow of majority carriers, so the junction can
be used as a "gate".
If the junction of forward biased by applying a positive voltage V to the p-region, as
presented in ﬁgure A.32, its potential is increased with respect to the n-region, so that an
electric ﬁeld is produced in a direction opposite to that of the built-in ﬁeld.
With the presence of the external bias voltage, a departure from equilibrium and a
misalignment of the Fermi levels in the p- and n-regions, as well as in the depletion layer,
occurs. The presence of two Fermi levels in the depletion layer, Efc and Efv, represents a
state of quasi-equilibrium.
The net eﬀect of the forward bias is to reduce the potential-energy by an amount of eV .
The net current becomes i = isexp(eV/kT)−is, with is as a constant. The excess majority
of carrier holes and electrons that enter the n- and p-regions, become minority carriers
and will recombine with the local majority carriers. Their concentration will decrease with
increasing distance from the junction.
151
==PAGE:162==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.32: Energy-band diagram and carrier concentrations for a forwards-
biased p −n junction. Figure from [6].
If the junction is reverse biased by applying a negative voltage V to the p-region,
the potential-energy is enhanced by eV .
The corresponding current is multiplied by
the exponential factor exp(eV/kT), where V is negative.
The net current is i =
isexp(ev/kT) −is, which means that a current of magnitude ≈is ﬂows in the reverse
direction when |V | ≫kT/e.
A p −n junction acts as a diode with a current-voltage (i −V ) characteristics of
i = is
"
exp
 
eV
kT
!
−1
#
(A.90)
A.7.11
The p −i −n Junction Diode
A p−i−n junction diode is made when a layer of intrinsic (or lightly doped) semiconductor
material is inserted between a p-type region and a n-type region, as seen in ﬁgure A.33.
As the depletion layer extends into each side of a junction, by a distance inversely
proportional to the doping concentration, the depletion layer of the p−i junction penetrates
deeply into the i-region. In the same manner, the depletion layer of the i −n junction
extends into the p-region. The p −i −n diode can behave like a p −n junction with a
depletion layer that generally refers to the entire intrinsic region.
The electron energy, density of ﬁxed charges and the electric ﬁeld in a p −i −n junction
diode in thermal equilibrium is presented are ﬁgure A.33.
An advantage of using a diode with a large depletion layer it its small junction capacitance
and therefore its fast response. Because so, p −i −n diodes are often favoured over p −n
diodes for use as semiconductor photodetectors, [6].
152
==PAGE:163==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.33: Electron energy, ﬁxed-charge density and electric ﬁeld magnitude
for a p −i −n junction diode in thermal equilibrium. Figure from [6].
A.7.12
Heterojunctions
Heterojuction are junction made up oﬀdiﬀerent semiconductor materials. The electron
similarities to the materials determine the alignments of the conduction- and valence-band
edges.
The juxtaposition of diﬀerent semiconductors can have many advantages, such as;
• To reduce the proportion of current carried by minority carriers, and thereby in-
creasing the injection eﬃciency, as seen in ﬁgure A.34. This is achieved by junction
materials of diﬀerent bandgaps, in order to create localized jumps in the energy-band
diagram. The potential-energy discontinuity will provide a barrier that can prevent
selected charge carriers from entering a region where they are not wanted.
• To conﬁne charge carriers to a desired region of space. The is achieved by disconti-
nuities in the energy-band diagram, which are created by two heterojunctions. An
example hereof could be a layer of narrow-band material, as shown in the p −p −n
structure presented in ﬁgure A.34, which consist of a p −p heterojunction and a
p −n heterojunction.
• To accelerate carriers at speciﬁc locations. This is achieved by the energy-band
discontinuities.
A.7.13
LED
The light-emitting diode (LED) is a forward-biased p −n junction, as presented in
subsection 2.8.1 The Biased p −n Junction. They are usually made up oﬀdirect-
bandgap semiconductors and they emit light by spontaneous emission, as presented in in
subsection 2.8.1 Light and Matter. As previous described, the electron-hole radiative
recombination results in the emission of light in the form of light from a semiconductor
material. The photon emission rate can be increased by the use of external means, in order
153
==PAGE:164==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.34: The p−p−n double heterojunction structure. The middle layer is
of a narrower bandgap than the outer layers. In equilibrium, the Fermi levels align
so that the edge of the conduction band drops sharply at the p −p junction and
the edge of the valence band drops sharply at the p −n junction. When the device
is forward biased, these jumps act as barriers that conﬁne the injected minority
carriers to the region of lower bandgap. Electrons injected from the n-region, for
example, are prevented from diﬀusing beyond the barrier at the p −p junction.
Similarly, holes injected from the p-region are not permitted to diﬀuse beyond
the energy barrier conﬁguration therefore forces electrons and holes to occupy
a narrow common region. This increases the eﬃciency of light-emitting diode,
semiconductor optical ampliﬁers and laser diodes. Figure from [6].
to increase the excess electron-hole pairs in the material. This may be accomplished by
illuminating the material, but it is typically achieved by forward biasing the p −n junction,
which injects carrier pairs into the junction region. This process is shown in ﬁgure A.35.
Figure A.35: Spontaneous photon emission resulting from electron-hole radiative
recombination, as might occur in a forward-biased p −n junction. Figure from [6].

semiconductor material, is directly proportional to the carrier-pair injection rate R, as
presented in ﬁgure A.35.

A.7.14
Semiconductor Laser
The underlying operation of a semiconductor laser is the creation of a population inversion
which renders stimulated emission more prevalent than absorption.
A semiconductor laser diode is a semiconductor optical ampliﬁer that features a path for
optical feedback. The semiconductor optical ampliﬁer is a forward-biased p −n junction
fabricated from a direct-bandgap semiconductor.
A.7.15
Laser Characteristics
The most relevant characteristics describing a laser, for the problem at hand, is presented
in the following paragraphs.
A.7.16
Continuous Wave Characteristics
The semiconductor laser operation is well described by a set of rate equations that govern
the interaction of photons and electrons inside the active region of the laser. The equations
describe the rates of change of the population densities as a result of pumping, radiative
and nonradiative transitions.

A.7.17
Pulsed Laser
A.7.18
Mode Locking
Mode locking diﬀers from the above presented approaches to generating pulsed lasers, in
that it is a dynamic steady-state process or an equilibrium mode distribution, where the
distribution of light is no longer changing with the ﬁbre length or with modal excitation,
[6]. The three other approaches are based in the transient dynamics of a laser medium. A
laser can oscillate on many longitudinal modes, with frequencies that are equally separated.
These modes do normally oscillate independently, but by the mode locking process they
can by external means be coupled and their phases can be locked together.
If each of the laser modes is approximated by a uniform plane wave propagating in the
z direction, with a velocity v = c/n, the total complex wavefunction of the ﬁeld can be
described as a sum

If M ≈∆ν/νF , then τpulse = TF /M ≈1/∆ν, as seen in ﬁgure A.39. The pulse duration
τpulse is inversely proportional to the atomic linewidth ∆ν. As ∆ν can be large, very
narrow mode-locked laser pulses can be generated, [6]. The ratio between the peak and
mean intensities is equal to the number of modes M.
As seen in ﬁgure A.39 each peak has a peak-intensity of Ip = M ¯I, a mean intensity of ¯I
and a pulse length of dpulse = 2d/M.
A.7.19
Quasi-continuous-wave Operation
Quasi-continuous-wave (quasi-cw) operation of a laser means that its pump source is
switched on only for certain time intervals, which are short enough to reduce thermal
eﬀects signiﬁcantly, but still long enough that the laser process is close to its steady state,
meaning the laser is optically in the state of continuous-wave operation, [97].

A.8.2
p −n Photodiodes
In ﬁgure A.40 the structure of a reverse-biased p −n junstion is presented.
A reverse-biased p−n junction consists of a depeltion region, which is devoid of free charge
carriers and a large built-in electric ﬁeld opposes the ﬂow of electrons from the n-side to
163
==PAGE:174==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.40: Schematically representation of a p −n photodiode under reverse
bias. Figure from [3].
the p-side and of holes from the p-side to the n-side. When a p −n junction is illuminated
with light on side, for example the p-side as seen in ﬁgure A.40, electron-hole pairs are
created through absorption. Due to the large build-in electric ﬁeld, electron and holes,
that are generated inside the depletion region, accelerate in opposite directions and drift
to the n- and p-sides, respectively, as seen in ﬁgure A.41. The resulting ﬂow of current is
proportional to the incident optical power and the reverse-bias p −n junctions acts as a
photodiode.
Figure A.41: Energy-band diagram, of a p −n photodiode, showing carrier
movement through drift and diﬀusion. Figure from [3].
The bandwidth is often limited by the transit time ttr, as seen in equation 2.30. With the
width of the depeltion region to be W and νd as the drift velocity, the transit time is given
as
τtr = W
νd
(A.137)
Typically values are W ≈10µm, νd ≈105 m/s and τ ≈100 ps, [3].
164
==PAGE:175==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.42: Equivalent RC circuit to calculation of the RC constant τRC.
Figure from [4].
The RC constant τRC of a equivalent RC circuit, as seen in ﬁgure A.42, in the bandwidth
equation 2.30, can be written as
τRC = (RL + Rs)Cp
(A.138)
where RL is the external load resistance, Rs is the internal series resistance and Cp is the
parasitic capacitance. A typical value of the RC constant is τRC ≈100 ps, [3].
The limiting factor for the bandwidth of the p−n photodiode are the presence of a diﬀusive
component in the photocurrent. This is related to the absorption of incident light outside
of the depeltion region. That is electrons generated in the p-region and holes generated
in the p-region. These electrons would have to diﬀuse to the depeltion-region boundary
before the can drift to the n-side and similarly, these holes would have to diﬀuse to the
depeltion-region boundary. In ﬁgure A.43 it is shown how the diﬀusive component can
distort the temporal response of a photodiode.
Figure A.43: Response of a p −n photodiode to a rectangular optical pulse when
both drift and diﬀusion contribute to the detector current. Figure from [3].
The contribution of the diﬀusion can be reduced by dereasing the widths of the p- and
n-regions and increasing the depletion region, so that most of the incident light is absorbed
165
==PAGE:176==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
in the depletion region. This is the approach adopted for p −i −n photodiodes.
A.8.3
p −i −n Photodiodes
As discussed in subsection A.8.2 increasing the depletion region can decrease the
contribution of diﬀusion. In a p −i −n junction a layer of nearly intrinsic material
is inset between the p −n junction.
In ﬁgure A.44 the structure of a p −i −n junction structure together with the electric-ﬁeld
distribution inside it under reverse-bias operation.
Figure A.44: p −i −n photodiode together with the electric-ﬁeld distribution
under reverse bias. Figure from [3].
As the intrinsic layer has a high resistance, and most of the voltage drops across it, a large
electric ﬁeld exists in the i-layer.
The depletion region extends throughout the i-region and its width W is controlled by
changing the thickness of the middle layer.
The main diﬀerence between the p −n photodiode and the p −i −n photodiode, is that
the drift component of the photocurrent dominates over the diﬀusion component, as most
of the incident power is absorbed in the i-region of the p −i −n photodiode [3].
The optimum width W is a compromise between responsivity and response time. The
responsivity can can increased by increasing W and thereby letting the quantum eﬃciency
η approach 100%, as presented in equation 2.28. However, the response time also increases
as the width W increases, because it takes longer for carriers to drift across the depletion
region.
A.8.4
Avalanche Photodiodes
As all detectors require a certain minimum current to operate, the current needed, as
seen in the photocurrent equation Ip, can be rewritten into a minimum power needed as
Pin = IP/Rd. Having a photodetector with a large responsivity Rd therefore require less
power and is more desirable. The responsivity of the p −i −n photodiode is limited by
the responsivity and has a minimum value of Rd = q/hν for η = 1.
For avalanche photodetectors (APDs) can have a larger value of Rd and can be used when
166
==PAGE:177==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
the optical power for the receiver is limited.
The avalanche photodetector provide an internal current gain and the physical phenomenon
behind is impact ionization, [3]. This stems from the fact that under certain conditions,
an accelerating electron can acquire suﬃcient energy to generate a new electron-hole pair.
Looking at the E −k band representation in ﬁgure A.24, the electron gives a parts of
its kinetic energy to another electron in the valence band and leaves behind a hole. The
net result from impact ionization is that a single primary electron, which is generated
through absorption of a photon, creates many secondary electrons and holes, which all
contribute to the photodiode current. In the same manner the primary hole can generate
secondary electron-hole pairs that also contributes to the photocurrent. The generations
rate is governed by the impact ionization coeﬃcients for electrons and holes, αe and αh,
respectively. The numerical vales of αe and αh depends on the semiconductor material
and the electric ﬁeld that accelerates the electrons and holes. In ﬁgure A.45 the αe and αh
coeﬃcients are presented for several semiconductors as a function of electric ﬁeld.
Figure A.45: Impact-ionization coeﬃcients for several semiconductors as a
function of the electric ﬁeld for electrons, the solid lines, and holes, the dashed
lines. Figure from [3].
Avalanche photodiodes has an additional layer, as compared to the p −i −n photodiode, in
which secondary electron-hole pairs are generated though impact ionization. In ﬁgure A.46
the APD structure is presented together with the variation of the electric ﬁeld though the
diﬀerent layers.
As seen in ﬁgure A.46, under reverse bias, a high electric ﬁeld exists in the p-type region,
which is placed between the i-region and the n+-region. This layer is the multiplication
layer, as it is here secondary electron-hole pairs are generated though impact ionization.
The i-region is still the depletion region, where most of the incident photons are absorbed
and primary electron-hole pairs are generated. The electrons generated in the i-region will
cross the gain region and generate secondary electron-hole pairs that are responsible for
the current gain.
167
==PAGE:178==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.46: An APD together with the electric-ﬁeld distribution inside the
diﬀerent layers under reverse bias. Figure from [3].
A.8.5
Receiver Sensitivity and Bit-Error Rate
A receiver is more sensitivity, as compared to another, if it achieves the same performance
with less optical power incident on it.
Receiver sensitivity can be deﬁned as the minimum average power needed to keep the BER
below a certain value. A commonly used value for digital optical receivers requires the
BER to be below 1 × 10−9, [3].
The sensitivity of the receiver is then deﬁned as the minimum average received power ¯Prec
required by the receiver to operate at a BER of 10−9.
Figure A.47: a) The ideal noiseless photocurrent. b) Same photocurrent but
distorted and contaminated by noise. The input threshold current ID is shown.
c) Reconstructed output data where the last bit is in error due to negative noise
ﬂuctuation. Figure from [13].
In ﬁgure A.47 a) the ideal noiseless photocurrent is shown. The photocurrent has not
been distorted by propagation in the ﬁbre or contaminated by noise. In ﬁgure A.47 b)
the real photocurrent I(t) is shown. The photocurrent is distorted and inﬂuenced by
noise. The input threshold current ID is shown. The threshold current being the current
168
==PAGE:179==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
level at which the incident photocurrent is detected as a 1 or a 0, being at a high level
or a low, respectively. In ﬁgure A.47 c) the reconstructed output data is shown. Here
the last bit is in error due to negative noise ﬂuctuations because the photocurrent is negative.
A decision circuit compares the input photocurrent with the threshold current and decides
for a 1 bit of I > ID or a 0 bit if I < ID. Errors occurs if I < ID for a 1 bit because of
receiver noise. An error can also occurs if I > ID for a bit 0.
The error probability can be expressed as
BER = p(1)P(0 | 1) + p(0)P(1 | 0)
(A.139)
where p(1) and p(0) are the probabilities for receiving a 1 or a 0 bit, respectively, P(0 | 1)
is the probability that the received 1 bit is erroneously detected as a 0 bit and P(1 | 0) is
the probability that the received 0 bit is erroneously detected as a 1 bit.
Assuming that 1 and 0 bit are equally likely to occur, so that p(1) = p(0) = 1/2 and the
BER becomes
BER = 1
2[P(0 | 1) + P(1 | 0)]
(A.140)
In ﬁgure A.48 a) the photocurrent I(t) contaminated by noise versus time is presented.
Figure A.48: a) Photocurrent for a 1 bit surrounded by two 0 bits. b) Probability
density function (pdf) for a 1 bit and a 0 bit. Figure from [13].
A 1 bit is shown, with its average high level current of I1 and the low level at I0. The
time slot is of duration TB = 1/B, where B is the bit rate, is also shown. The bit is
sampled in the middle of the time slot. Due to noise, the sampled high level current is a
stochastic variable with the probability density function (pdf) as shown in ﬁgure A.48 b).
The average value for the 1 bit is
I1 = RP1 + Id

Minimum Received Power

with a BER below a certain value, for instance 10−9 can be calculated.

incident optical power. It is assumed that 0 bits carry no optical power, so that P0 = 0
and thus I0 = 0. The power in 1 bits is related to I1 as

A.9
Loss Management
A.9.1
Erbium-Doped Fibre Ampliﬁers (EDFA)
Erbium-doped ﬁbre ampliﬁers are made with a gain medium, created by doping the ﬁbre
core with rare-earth elements, when manufactured. The core inside an EFDA contains
erbium ions (Er3+). EDFAs achieve ampliﬁcation through stimulated emission, which
is presented in subsection 2.8.1 Light and Matter. By pumping the erbium ions, at a
suitable wavelength, then by means of stimulated emission, population inversion is achieved,
which provide optical gain. The amorphous nature of silica broadens the energy levels of
Er3+ into bands, and a few of the energy bands are presented in ﬁgure A.50
As seen in ﬁgure A.50, many transitions can be used to pump an EDFA to possible
absorption and stimulated emission transitions. The most eﬃcient pumping wavelengths,
for pumping to a wavelength of 1550 nm, are 1480 nm and 980 nm, all presented with red
in ﬁgure A.50.
A simpliﬁed energy band diagram is presented in ﬁgure A.51, with a 980 nm pump. The
pump photon stimulates an electron, in the optical ﬁbre, to a higher energy level, from
where it drops instantaneously in a non-radiative process, to a lower energy level. In this
energy level population inversion is achieved, and electron will drop to the ground level
173
==PAGE:184==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.50: Energy-level diagram of erbium ions in silica ﬁbres. Figure from
[4].
again. In the drop a photon of the desired wavelength of 1550 nm is released, though
stimulated emission.
Figure A.51: Simpliﬁed energy level diagram for a 980 nm pump. Figure from
[4].
For the case of the 1480 nm pump population inversion is also achieved due to the many
energy levels in Er3+, keeping in mind that ﬁgure A.50 is a simpliﬁed energy-band diagram.
EDFAs can be designed in such a way that the pump and signal beams propagates in the
same direction, known as forward pumping. They can also propagate in opposite directions,
known as backward pumping. The two pumping directions can also be used together as
bidirectional pumping, which is presented in ﬁgure A.52.
Using the 980 nm wavelength pump gives a population inversion of 100 %, where as using
the 1480 nm wavelength pump only give a population inversion of 70 %. The 980 nm
pump will give a high gain for a short EDF, where the 1480 nm pump will give a high
174
==PAGE:185==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
Figure A.52:
EDFAs can be designed in pump-conﬁgurations of forward,
backward or bi-directional. Figure from [4].
gain for a longer EDF. The 980 nm pump has a low noise ﬁgure, Fn, where the 1480 nm
pump has a high noise ﬁgure.

A.9.2
Raman Ampliﬁers
The Raman ampliﬁer make use of stimulated Raman scattering, as presented in subsection
2.5.3. The gain in Raman ampliﬁers uses the Raman shift, given as ΩR = ωp −ωs. The
maximum gain occurs at ΩR = 13 THz and the value of the peak of the gain gR for a
wavelength of 1550 nm is gR = 6 · 10−13 m/W, all presented in subsection 2.5.3.
The Raman gain coeﬃcient is related to optical gain as g = gRIp, where Ip is the pump
intensity, [3]. In terms of pump power Pp, the gain can be expressed as
g(z) = gR(ω)Ip = gR
Pp
ap
(A.173)
where ap is the cross-sectional area of the laser beam inside the ﬁbre and the ratio gR/ap
is a measure of the Raman-gain eﬃciency.
The application schemes for Rman ampliﬁers are either lumped or distributed. In the
lumped scheme, a device is made up of spooled specially doped ﬁbre, that can enhance
the Raman gain. The ﬁbre is pumped and ampliﬁcation is achieved. For the distributed
scheme, the same ﬁbre used for transmission is used for signal ampliﬁcation. Pump light is
usually injected in the backward direction. The main drawback of both schemes is the
175
==PAGE:186==
A. Elaboration and Detailed Calculations
high power required.

is used to amplify a CW signal. The variation in the pump and in the signal, along the
length of the ampliﬁer, can be seen from the coupled equations presented in subsection
2.5.3. In terms of pump and signal power, these equations will take the form, [3]

C ORIGINAL PROJECT DESCRIPTION
The original project description is presented below.
180
==PAGE:191==
 Optical fibres for fault detection in long submarine power cables 
Master project 
Background 
Energinet.dk plans a 1400MW, 525kV, HVDC connection between Great Britain and Denmark. The project is 
being jointly developed and funded by National Grid and Energinet.dk. The connection will consist of two 
submarine and underground cables and two converter stations. The length of the connection is 760km 
(630km subsea) between Great Britain and Denmark [VikingLink supplier information pack presentation 
presented by Energinet.dk]. 
Submarine cables of this length can be expected to experience several faults through their lifetime. 
Monitoring of such cable systems can be done by means of optical fibres and the question is what the 
capabilities and limitations are of the fibres and also what impact cable faults might have on them. 
 
Project description 
The objective of this master project is to investigate to what degree optical fibres can be used in connection 
with fault detection in submarine power cables. 
In the project the necessary background information will be given about the optical fibre, such as types, 
characteristics, the process of transmitting and receiving a signal and the optical network components 
required for transmission over long distances. 
Investigations of the response of optical fibres to different physical exposures under cables faults is the main 
idea of the project. This includes detection both under and after the fault. State-of-the-art within different 
methods, their properties, limitations and application range will be given. 
Main focus of the investigations is expected to be on Optical Time Domain Reflectometers (OTDR) and the 
optical fibre working as a temperature sensor. 
The work will cover both literature studies, analytical approaches to promising solutions and proposals of 
realistic solutions. Simulations of the proposed solutions will be made to investigate the different solutions in 
particular with respect to, sensitivity, range, monitoring and localization potential. These findings can be 
validated by experimental investigations under laboratory conditions. 
All the aspects and findings during the project are to be documented in a final report. 
The project includes: 
• 
A literature review covering: 
◦ 
Background information about the optical fibre, including type, characteristic, the process of 
transmitting and receiving a signal, and the components of an optical network 
◦ 
Optical fibres sensitivity to changes in the physical environment 
◦ 
State-of-the-art within different methods of optical fibres used for monitoring and fault 
localisation, their properties, limitations and application range 
• 
Solutions and proposals 
◦ 
Identification of possible optical fibres, signal type and network components 
◦ 
Solutions utilizing dispersion, nonlinearities, losses and noise 
◦ 
Simulation of the solutions with respect to sensitivity, range, monitoring and localization potential 
◦ 
Proposals for realistic solutions in submarine power cables  
 
• 
Reporting 
Regular meetings will be held between the project partners, in order to ensure focus on results, which can 
form background for future recommendations of choice of detection system.
